In a week, it will be in every university in Germany. There will be no taking it back.”

I stared at the form in his hands. My father’s beautiful, perilous script was being translated into this army of lead. Multiplied. Made immortal and vulnerable all at once. This was the moment. The spiritual seeking had become a physical act of rebellion, and I was its witness.

“Why?” I heard myself ask. “Why show me this?”

“Because you are not just a messenger,” he said. “You are the first convert. I saw it in your eyes at the grate. You carry the weight of it not as a burden, but as a weapon you are learning to hold. You need to see the forge where the weapons are made.”

He handed me the composing stick. “Here. Feel it.”

The metal was cool, heavy. The reversed letters were a cryptic, miniature landscape. This was the source. Not the parchment, not the ink, but this leaden truth. I felt a strange power in it.

“They will come for him,” I said quietly. “The author. And for you.”

“Let them come,” Luther said, and there was no boast in it, only a flat, factual readiness. “The truth is a fire. It will burn them, or it will warm the world. There is no middle ground.” He took the stick back from me. “Now you must go. Georg will take you back. When the pamphlet is printed, you will take copies. North, to Rostock. South, to Basel. You will be my… distributors. Your father can go home to his scriptorium. But you, Thomas… you have left that road. This is your road now. The road of the word.”

He said my name. He had plucked it from the air, from my unspoken self. In that moment, I was no longer the parchment-seller’s son. I was Thomas, who carried fire.

Georg led me back through the dripping alleyways, through the gap in the wall, back to the silent guest room. My father still slept. I lay on my pallet, the smell of ink on my hands, the clink-clink-clink of the lead type echoing in my skull. I had found my new strength. It was not a sword or a secret. It was a purpose, hard and clear as lead type. I was part of the machine that would shout the dangerous truth into the silent face of the world.

Dawn came, grey and wet. A different lay brother brought us watery porridge. As we ate, the door opened and Luther entered, once more in his black habit, the fierce energy of the print shop banked behind a mask of monastic calm. He carried a small, oilcloth-wrapped bundle.

“The sample has been examined,” he said, his voice formal, for any listening ears. “It is of interest. Here is your payment for its delivery.” He handed the bundle to my father. It was heavy. Not with coin, but with paper. “And a letter of introduction to the stationer’s guild in Erfurt, praising your quality. You should return home with all speed.”

My father took it, his hands trembling with relief. He heard the dismissal, the safe exit offered. He nodded, bowing. “Thank you, Father. Thank you.”

Luther’s eyes met mine over my father’s bent head. “Go with God.”

We were ushered out the same way we had entered. The door in the high wall closed behind us, and we stood again in the mundane, muddy street. The world had not ended. The sun was trying to break through the clouds. It was over.

“We are free,” my father breathed, clutching the oilcloth bundle to his chest as if it were a holy relic. “We are alive. We go home.”

We walked toward the city gate, joining the stream of carts and pilgrims. The spiritual seeking was done. My father’s face began to lose its grey fear, softening with the prospect of his familiar desk, his quiet inks. He had his truth: survival.

I walked beside him, but I was already gone. I carried a different truth. In the oilcloth bundle were fifty printed pamphlets. Disputatio pro declaratione virtutis indulgentiarum. By Martin Luther. The theses, my father’s script, transformed into a weapon anyone could hold. My road was not back to Erfurt. It was to the north, and the south, a road of whispers and danger. I had faced the door, I had seen the fire forged, and I had been forged in it, too.

We reached the city gate. My father turned to me, a smile touching his lips for the first time in days. “We did it, Thomas. We are safe.”

I looked at his face, so full of simple, earned peace. I looked at the road ahead, one fork leading east to home, the other leading north into the unknown.

I hefted my own pack, which held my share of the fire. “Yes, Father,” I said. “We are safe.”

And as we passed under the shadow of the gate, I made my choice.

Chapter 7

CHAPTER SEVEN

The road north was a different kind of pilgrimage. It was not made of mud and penitent hope, but of hard-packed earth and the sharp, secret purpose of a man who carries a spark into dry tinder. My pack held my share of the fire: twenty-five of the pamphlets, wrapped in the same oilcloth that had carried them from Luther’s hand to my father’s. The other twenty-five were with my father, heading east to Erfurt, to be sold as simple stationer’s work to men who would not know they held a lit fuse. I carried the knowledge. He carried the merchandise. The division felt like a crack in the world.

I had left him at the fork outside Wittenberg’s eastern gate. His relief was a solid, warm thing, a hearth I could still feel at my back. My own path was a cold front, a gathering wind.

“You will write?” he had asked, his hand on my shoulder.

“When I can,” I had said. The lie was a stone in my throat. Words on a page would be a trail, and trails could be followed.

He had nodded, believing, because fathers must believe their sons. He had pressed a small purse into my hand—half the fee from the abbot, he said, for my work. Then he had turned and walked toward the familiar, his step already lighter, the terrible weight of the prior’s commission lifted. I watched him until he was a smudge against the road, until the distance between us was no longer measured in paces but in kind. He was going home to ink and vellum. I was walking into the print-smoke.

I walked for two days, my feet finding their old, stubborn rhythm. The pain was a companion now, a familiar bitterness. The landscape changed, the flat Saxon water-meadows giving way to gentle, wooded hills. The spiritual seeking was done, Luther had said. But he was wrong. My seeking had only changed its object. Before, I had sought an answer, a truth to hold against the dark. Now I sought the mouths that would speak it, the ears that would hear it, the hands that would pass it on. It was a seeking of a different, darker sacrament.

1.

The town of Jüterbog announced itself with the smell of burning charcoal and the shriek of sawmills. It was a town of industry, not theology, its wealth hewn from the dark forests that crowded its edges. The stationer’s shop was on the square, a narrow wedge of a building squeezed between a butcher and a cooper. The sign above the door showed a quill and an inkpot, faded by sun and rain.

I pushed the door open, a bell jangling a thin, metallic alarm above me. The air inside was thick with the smells I knew best: paper, glue, the peppery scent of oak-gall ink, and beneath it, the newer, oilier smell of printer’s ink. A different sacrament.

A man looked up from a bench where he was stitching a gathering of pages. He was my father’s age, but where my father was worn soft like old vellum, this man was sharp—sharp nose, sharp eyes behind wire spectacles, sharp attention that pricked at me like a needle.

“You’re from Wittenberg,” he said. It wasn’t a question.

“I am.” I slid the pack from my shoulder, the movement deliberate. “I have a delivery. From the Augustinian cloister.”

His hands stilled. He did not ask from whom. He simply wiped his fingers on his apron, leaving grey smudges, and came around the bench. He locked the shop door and turned the sign to closed. The daylight from the window seemed to dim, concentrating in the dust motes dancing between us.

“Show me.”

I unwrapped the oilcloth on his clean worktable. The pamphlets lay there, a stack of potential. The title, in my father’s flawless textura, was a stark black banner: Disputatio pro declaratione virtutis indulgentiarum.

The stationer—his name was Kohler—did not touch them at first. He leaned close, his spectacles glinting, and read the first lines. His lips moved silently. A slow breath hissed out between his teeth, a sound like a blade being drawn from a sheath.

“So. It is true. The monk has gone from whisper to shout.” He finally picked one up, feeling the weight of the paper, the crispness of the impression. “This is good work. Excellent work. The register is perfect. Your father’s hand?”

“Yes.”

“He always was the best.” Kohler set the pamphlet down with a reverence that bordered on fear. “And you are to be my… distributor?”

“I am to see them placed,” I said, echoing the instructions given to me by the silent brother who had met me at the stable yard the morning I left. “In the right hands. For the right price.”

Kohler’s sharp eyes assessed me. “You understand what you carry? This is not a shipment of psalters. This is a declaration of war, printed on paper. Tetzel will scream heresy. The archbishop will demand blood. The pope will hear of it, if he hasn’t already.”

I felt the weight of the oilcloth bundle in my father’s hands. We are free. We are alive. “I understand.”

He nodded, a quick, bird-like motion. “Good. Then here is your tangible reward.” He went to a strongbox behind his bench, worked a key, and lifted out a smaller, much heavier pouch. It clinked with the solid, undeniable music of coin. He placed it on the table beside the pamphlets. “For the delivery. And a percentage of what these bring. I have buyers. Clerics with doubts, merchants with grievances, scholars with curiosity. The hungry.”

My hand closed over the pouch. It was more money than I had ever held. It was payment for setting a fire. The spiritual seeking had ended at the door of the Black Cloister. This was a commercial transaction. It felt infinitely more profane.

“There is more,” Kohler said. He slid a single, folded sheet from beneath his blotter. It was a list, written in a tight, secretive hand. Names. Towns. Leipzig. Magdeburg. Nuremberg. Berlin. “These are men of the trade. Stationers, printers, book sellers. They are… receptive. They have networks. You will travel. You will speak to them. You will be our thread.”

I took the list. The paper was smooth, expensive. The names were a map of a new, hidden Germany. My road was no longer a line north; it was a web.

“I am a thread,” I repeated, the word feeling thin and dangerous in my mouth.

“You are a courier of light,” Kohler corrected, but his tone was that of a man balancing ledgers. “Or of fire. Depends on which end you stand. Now, go out the back. The butcher’s boy sometimes watches my front for the bishop’s men. They pay him in sausages.”

He rewrapped the pamphlets in plain brown paper, tied them with string, and handed them back to me. My pack was heavier now, with coin and conspiracy. As I turned to the rear door that led to a stinking alley, he spoke again.

“Brother Thomas.”

I stopped. He never asked my name. He knew.

“The indulgence preachers are not just selling slips of paper. They are selling a story. A story of fear, and a pope who holds the keys to escape it. You…” He tapped the brown-wrapped package in my arms. “You are now selling a different story. Stories are the only things men truly fight for. Remember that when they come for you.”

2.

I found Ulrich at the inn he’d named, The Forest Crown, a low, smoky building on the edge of Jüterbog where the woodcutters and charcoal-burners drank. He was in a corner, a massive shape hunched over a tankard, his presence carving a space of quiet in the rowdy room. He saw me and lifted a hand, two fingers raised. Our signal.

I sat on the stool opposite him, the pack between my feet. The noise of the inn washed over us—raucous laughter, the thump of tankards, a fiddler scraping out a tune. It was a celebration of a day’s hard work done, of muscles eased by ale. Our victory was quieter, more fragile.

“You saw the sharp man?” Ulrich asked, his voice a low rumble beneath the din.

“I saw him.” I patted the pack. “It is done.”

A serving girl brought me ale. Ulrich waited until she left, then leaned forward. “And?”

I told him of Kohler, of the list, of the web. I did not mention the coin. Some truths are too heavy to share, even with a brother. He listened, his eyes on the door, the windows, the crowd. He was always watching.

“Good,” he grunted when I finished. “A web is harder to tear than a chain.” He took a long drink. “We celebrate, then. A small victory. The word is moving.”

We touched tankards. The ale was thin and sour, but it was a ritual. We had done it. We had taken Luther’s fire from the cloister and given it to the world. The spiritual seeking had borne fruit, tangible, printable fruit. For a moment, sitting in the warm, noisy dark, I felt a sliver of my father’s peace. We were alive. We were succeeding.

Ulrich leaned back, a faint smile on his bearded face. “I have news of my own. While you were with the sharp man, I listened. The woodcutters talk. Tetzel left Wittenberg in a black rage. But he did not go south to report his failure. He went east, toward Leipzig.”

I felt the sliver of peace turn cold. “Leipzig is on the list.”

Ulrich’s smile vanished. “He travels with his usual retinue. Dominican guards. A scribe. And two new faces, they say. Men in travel-stained clothes who ask no questions and watch everything.”

“The archbishop’s men,” I said. The consequence of our ordeal was not past; it was following us, taking new shape.

“Or the pope’s,” Ulrich said. “It makes no difference. They are hunters. And we have just laid a scent trail.” He nodded at my pack. “You carry it with you. The sharp man’s web is a trail. They will know someone is distributing. They will look for a courier.”

The celebration was over, crushed under the weight of the new danger. The noise of the inn felt suddenly hostile, a mask hiding watching eyes. I saw the butcher’s boy, paid in sausages. How many other eyes were for sale?

“We leave tonight,” I said.

Ulrich shook his head. “You leave tonight. I will stay, provide a different scent. A big man, buying pamphlets, asking loud questions. I will lead them on a merry chase through the taverns. You will slip out like a ghost.”

It was a good plan. It put him in the path of the hunters. I began to protest, but he cut me off.

“This is the fight, Brother Thomas. Not just carrying the word. Protecting it. This is the consequence. You chose it under the gate. Now live it.”

He was right. I had made my choice. The celebration was a fleeting illusion. The ordeal was continuous. I finished my ale, the sour taste now one of acceptance.

“God go with you, Ulrich.”

“And with your spirit,” he said, raising his tankard in a final, grim salute. “Now go. Out the back. The stables. Take the grey mare. She is paid for and she is fast. Ride for Leipzig. Find the name on your list. And do not look back.”

3.

The grey mare was indeed fast, and she had a ground-eating canter that devoured the dark miles. I rode through the night, the forest a black wall on either side of the road, the only sound the drum of hooves, the rush of wind, and the hammering of my own heart. I did not look back. But I felt the pursuit as a cold pressure between my shoulder blades, a sixth sense honed by fear.

They found me at dawn, not on the road, but at the river crossing.

The Elbe was a silver scar in the morning mist. The ferry was a raft, poled by a sleepy old man. I was halfway across, the mare skittish on the unsteady platform, when I saw them. Three riders on the bank I had just left. They were still, watching. Even at that distance, I could see they were not pilgrims. They sat their horses too straight, with a military stiffness. One of them pointed directly at me.

The ferryman saw them too. He spat into the brown water. “Bishop’s men,” he muttered, heaving on his pole. “Or someone’s men. Trouble, either way.”

My hand went to my pack, slung across the mare’s withers. The pamphlets were there. The list was there. The coin was there. Evidence and motive.

“Can you go faster?” I asked, my voice tight.

“The river goes at its own speed,” he grumbled, but he leaned harder into the pole.

The far bank seemed to crawl toward us. The three riders did not move. They were waiting. They knew the ferry was the only crossing for miles. They had me in a bottle.

As the raft bumped against the landing, I threw the ferryman a coin from my purse—too much, but speed was worth gold—and kicked the mare onto the bank. She scrambled up the muddy slope. I looked back. The three riders were now moving, descending to the ferry landing. They would be across in minutes.

Leipzig was still a day’s ride. I could not outrun them on the open road. My father’s face flashed in my mind, soft with peace. We are safe. I had chosen the road that made a liar of me.

I turned the mare off the main track and plunged into the forest. It was ancient, thick with beech and oak, the canopy swallowing the morning light. I rode hard, ducking low branches, the mare responding with snorts of alarm. I was not a woodsman. I left a trail a blind man could follow: broken twigs, deep hoofprints in the soft loam.

After an hour of frantic riding, I had to stop. The mare was lathered, her sides heaving. I listened. At first, nothing but the drip of moisture from leaves, the call of a jay. Then, faint but unmistakable, the sound of a horn. Short, sharp blasts. Signals. They had dogs.

Panic, cold and pure, washed over me. The spiritual seeking had led me here, to be hunted like a stag through the greenwood. I fumbled with my pack, my numb fingers tearing at the straps. I had to get rid of the evidence. But as I pulled out the oilcloth bundle, my hands stilled. These were not just papers. They were the fire. To burn them or bury them was to surrender, to admit the story of fear was stronger.

The horn sounded again, closer.

I looked around wildly. Ahead, the ground sloped down toward the gurgle of water—a stream. An idea, desperate and fragile, formed. I urged the mare forward, down to the water’s edge. The stream was shallow, running over a bed of stones. I dismounted, my boots sinking into the icy water. I unwrapped the pamphlets. I took ten of them, the ones on top. I shoved the rest, along with the list and the heavy purse, deep into my pack. Then I mounted again.

Riding in the middle of the stream, I began to tear the ten pamphlets into pieces—not small, but large, legible fragments. I scattered them like sinful breadcrumbs, letting them float and catch on rocks, on roots at the water’s edge. A trail of heresy, leading downstream.

Then, at a bend, I turned the mare and forced her up the opposite bank, leaving the stream behind. I rode now with painstaking care, choosing rocky ground, staying under the thickest cover. I rode not for Leipzig, but west, back toward the road I had fled, a dangerous doubling back.

I heard the baying of hounds, excited, downstream where my paper trail led them. I heard shouted voices, muffled by the trees and the water. They had taken the bait.

I rode until the sounds faded, until the only pursuit was the thunder of my own blood in my ears. I found a hollow under the roots of a lightning-blasted oak, a dank cave of earth and rot. There, I hid my pack, covering it with leaves and deadfall. I kept only a few coins and a single, folded pamphlet inside my tunic.

The antagonist’s forces had regrouped. They had reacted. They had chased me to the edge of capture. And I had escaped by sacrificing a tenth of the fire. It felt like a defeat dressed in the rags of survival.

4.

I walked into Leipzig at dusk, a man on foot, leading a tired mare. I was just another traveler, dust on my boots, emptiness in my eyes. The city was a larger, louder, dirtier version of Wittenberg, a mercantile beast whose heart was the marketplace. I found the address on my list, a printer’s shop near the university. The sign showed a press and a sheaf of paper.

I stood across the street, watching. The shop was closed, shutters drawn. It looked quiet. Normal. But after Jüterbog, after the forest, I knew normal was a skin over a rotting body. The reward—the coin, the network, the tangible proof of our victory—was meaningless if I was caught. If the word was stopped. The larger problem was not the distribution; it was the machine that sought to grind the distribution to pulp. The archbishop, Tetzel, the men with dogs and horns. They were the larger problem. And I had run from them.

I had thought carrying the fire was the fight. Luther had faced the door. I had only faced a ferry crossing and run into the woods. The spiritual seeking had not ended with possession of the truth. It had transformed into the need to defend it, to become not just a courier, but a soldier in a war fought with paper and ink.

I could not just deliver the pamphlets here and move on to the next name on the list. That was the old plan, the plan of a man who thought the reward was the end. The hunters knew that plan now. They would be waiting at the next shop, and the next.

I had to finish the fight here. I had to turn their pursuit against them.

I needed a new story.

I stabled the mare at a different inn, one far from the printer’s shop. In the common room, over a bowl of greasy stew, I listened. The talk was of trade, of the upcoming fair, of a controversial debate scheduled at the university between a theologian from Ingolstadt and a local scholar. And, in lowered tones, of the famous indulgence preacher, Tetzel, who was in the city, lodged at the Dominican cloister, and who was said to be in a fury, vowing to root out a “pestilence of printed lies.”

He was here. The heart of the machine was here.

I finished my stew, the food like ash in my mouth. I went up to the tiny room I had rented, its window overlooking a tangle of rooftops. I took out the single pamphlet from my tunic. I smoothed it on the rough blanket. Disputatio pro declaratione virtutis indulgentiarum.

I had run from the hunters in the forest. I could not run from this. The reward alone—the successful delivery—had not solved the larger problem. It had amplified it. To truly protect the fire, I had to confront the wind that sought to blow it out.

I had to get the pamphlet into the hands of the scholar debating Tetzel’s ally. I had to seed it among the students at the university. I had to make sure that when Tetzel stood to defend his indulgences, the alternative story was already whispering in the halls.

It was no longer about distribution. It was about confrontation.

I blew out the candle and lay in the dark, listening to the sounds of the city. Somewhere, in a Dominican cell, Johann Tetzel was plotting his counterattack. Somewhere, in a forest, men with dogs were cursing over sodden paper fragments. And somewhere to the east, my father sat at his quiet desk, believing his son was safe.

I had made my choice under the gate at Wittenberg. Now, in Leipzig, I had to live the full consequence of it. The spiritual seeking had brought me to the truth. The living of it would require that I become a weapon in truth’s hand.

Tomorrow, I would not deliver. I would infiltrate. I would not hide. I would provoke.

The hook was set, not for a fish, but for a dragon. And I was the bait.

In the darkness, I finally understood Luther’s look as he said Go with God. It was not a blessing. It was a warning. You are going into the place where God’s presence is a battlefield, and you are now a soldier. Sleep, if you can.

I could not. The larger problem loomed, and I was walking toward it, a single pamphlet burning a hole against my heart.

Chapter 8

CHAPTER EIGHT

The mud of Leipzig was a different beast than Wittenberg’s. It was not the pilgrim-sucking mire of a road, but the greasy, soot-stained sludge of a city that chewed on people and spat out the bones. It clung to my boots with a proprietary claim, as if marking me for its own. The rain here was a fine, persistent drizzle, a tailor’s needle stitching a grey shroud over the rooftops.

We moved through the pre-dawn gloom not as a team, but as a conspiracy of three shadows. Ulrich was a mountain made animate, his silence a heavier thing than the damp air. Beside him, Elsa moved with the sharp, efficient grace of a stoat, her eyes missing nothing—the shuttered window, the drunk slumped in a doorway, the distant bark of a dog. She had appeared at the stable yard as I’d saddled the nag, saying nothing, simply falling into step. Her presence was neither comfort nor burden; it was a fact, like the knife I knew was sheathed at her wrist.

The pamphlet was a live coal in the inner pocket of my tunic. Not the single copy from my room—that one was ash now, burned in the grate, its words memorized. This was another, one of five I had separated from the main bundle. The rest were hidden in a place not even Ulrich knew. To carry all was to carry a pyre on my back.

“The Black Eagle,” Ulrich grunted, nodding toward a timber-framed inn that leaned over the narrow street like a gossiper. “Scholar’s haunt. He drinks there. The one debating Tetzel’s lapdog.”

I knew. I had spent the previous evening in its smoky common room, listening. Dr. Andreas Bodenstein. Karlstadt. A man of fire himself, but of a different kind—academic, theoretical. He would use the pamphlet as a weapon in a debate. I needed it to be a spark in a powder keg.

“You feel it?” Ulrich asked, his voice the low rumble of a cart over cobbles.

I did. It was the same feeling as standing in the forest before the dogs came. A pressure in the air, a sense of being measured by an unseen eye. The city was not sleeping. It was holding its breath.

“They know we’re here,” Elsa said, not looking at us. “Not who. Not why. But they know something is wrong in their garden.”

The Dominican cells were not far. Tetzel’s presence was a stain on the atmosphere, a spiritual stench of corruption and fear that even the woodsmoke couldn’t mask. My father’s quiet desk felt a continent away. The choice under the Wittenberg gate was a stone in my gut, permanent, irrevocable. The spiritual seeking was over. Now came the war of attrition.

We crossed the market square. The stalls were being erected, a skeleton of a marketplace emerging from the fog. A butcher flung a bucket of offal into the gutter, the smell a physical blow. I saw the man then, lingering by the fountain. He was too clean for this hour, his cloak too good, his posture too still. He was not looking for a market bargain. He was reading the crowd, page by page.

Ulrich saw him a heartbeat later. A subtle shift in his shoulders, the way a bull gathers itself before a charge. “Go,” he murmured. “The Eagle. We are the distraction.”

Elsa melted into an alley mouth without a sound. I hesitated, the coal of the pamphlet burning against my heart. To run felt like a failure of the resolve I had forged in the dark.

“Go,” Ulrich repeated, and there was the ghost of Luther’s warning in it. You are a soldier. This is the battlefield. “The seed is your task. The threshing is ours.”

He turned and walked, not away, but directly toward the clean man by the fountain, his size making him a moving hill, impossible to ignore. I turned and walked, not with haste, but with the deliberate pace of a student late for a lecture, into the mouth of a lane that led toward the university quarter.

The chase was not with dogs and torches. It was a chess game played on slick cobbles, with pieces of flesh and fear.

I heard the shout from the square—Ulrich’s voice, a roared insult about the price of indulgences and the weight of a Dominican’s purse. A provocation. The game was begun.

I reached the Black Eagle as the sky lightened to the color of a old bruise. I pushed inside, into the warmth of stale beer and unwashed wool. The common room was empty but for a serving boy sleeping on a bench. I made for the stairs. Karlstadt had a room on the upper floor; I had learned that, too, from the loose talk of drunk scholars.

I was on the third step when the door behind me opened again, letting in a slice of damp grey light and two men. They were not scholars. They wore the tough, serviceable clothes of men who did hard things for pay. Their eyes swept the room and landed on me, a stranger on the stairs at an hour too early for visiting.

I took the steps two at a time, the old wood groaning in protest. A shout from below. Footfalls, heavy and quick, following.

The upper corridor was a tunnel of closed doors. I knew which one was his—the third on the left, with a crude charcoal drawing of an owl on the plank. I reached it, hammered on the wood. “Doctor! A word, in the name of truth!”

From below, the sound of a bench overturning. The serving boy yelping.

The door did not open. I pounded again, my heart a frantic drum against the pamphlet’s coal. “Disputatio pro declaratione virtutis indulgentiarum! I have it!”

A bolt shot back. The door opened a crack, revealing a slice of a narrow, intelligent face, eyes puffy with sleep and study. “Who are you to shout such things at this hour?”

I shoved the pamphlet through the gap. “The wind to fan your fire, Doctor. Read it. Before Tetzel’s man silences you with dogma.”

I did not wait for his reply. I turned as the two men gained the top of the stairs. Their faces were flat, purposeful. No weapons drawn, not here, not yet. They wanted the pamphlet. They wanted me.

I ran the other way, toward the end of the corridor and a small, grimy window. It was not escape. It was the selection of a different battlefield. I threw the latch and swung it open. A two-story drop to a midden heap in a narrow courtyard. The smell rose like a wall.

One of the men lunged, fingers grazing my cloak. I did not think. I swung a leg over the sill and let myself fall.

The fall was a moment of pure, silent surrender. Then the midden heap took me, a wet, yielding crash that drove the air from my lungs. The stench was profound, a thick soup of rot and human waste. I rolled, gagging, coming up onto my knees in the filth.

A face appeared in the window above. Then another. They would come back down, around. I had minutes.

I scrambled across the courtyard, a reeking, stumbling thing, and found a low gate. It opened into another alley, this one strewn with broken casks. My breath sawed in my throat. The pain in my feet, the old companion from the pilgrim road, woke with a vengeance. I was a block of dull pain again, but moving, always moving, because to stop was to become a feature of this fouler landscape.

I had seeded the pamphlet. Karlstadt had it. The first part was done. Now I had to lead the hounds away from Ulrich and Elsa, away from the hidden bundle. I had to be the bait that kept the dragon’s gaze fixed on me.

They were clever. They did not simply chase. They herded.

I burst from the alley onto a slightly broader street, one leading toward the Pleissenburg, the city’s castle. A cart was ahead, loaded with hay. A man stepped from a doorway to my right. Not one of the two from the inn. A third. They were converging.

I ducked left, into the covered entrance of a chapel. The cold, damp silence within was a shock. A single candle burned at the altar. I staggered down the side aisle, my filthy boots leaving dark prints on the stone. I could hide here, in the shadows of a pillar, but it was a trap. A door at the far end, perhaps.

I heard the soft scrape of a shoe at the entrance. They were not afraid of God’s house. Why would they be? They served Tetzel. They believed they were God’s house.

I ran for the small door behind the altar. It was locked. I was in a stone bottle.

The footsteps came slowly, confidently, down the center aisle. One set. Then another, entering from a side door. They had me.

I turned, my back against the cold wood. The two men from the inn stood ten paces away, blocking the way to the world. The clean man from the fountain stood behind them, his face now visible in the candlelight. He had the calm, empty eyes of a clerk balancing a ledger.

“The pamphlet,” the clean man said. His voice was soft, polite. “And the others. And you will come with us to answer some questions for Father Tetzel. There is a reward for your cooperation. And for your lack of it, there are other methods.”

My hand went to my tunic. The single pamphlet was there. The others were not. The reward I had already spent, buying Karlstadt’s attention. The larger problem stood before me, smelling of rosewater and iron.

“I have only this,” I said, my voice echoing in the hollow space.

“Then you are of limited value,” the man said. He nodded to the two brutes. “Extract the location of the rest. The Father wishes to burn them himself.”

They advanced. The spiritual seeking had brought me to truth in a quiet study in Wittenberg. The living of it brought me to this: filth-soaked, cornered in a stolen moment of prayer, with only violence as my liturgy.

I braced. I would give them a fight. It would be short, and it would end with me broken in that ledger, a negative sum. But it would be time. Time for Ulrich. Time for Elsa. Time for the one pamphlet in Karlstadt’s hands to do its work.

As the first man lunged, the side door I had not noticed—a confessional booth—swung open.

Elsa did not emerge. She flowed.

The knife in her hand was a dart of silver in the candlelight. It took the first brute in the side of his thigh, not to kill, but to cripple. He roared, stumbling into his companion. The clean man’s eyes widened, his calculation disrupted.

Elsa was between them and me, a slight figure in the vast gloom. “The window,” she hissed at me. “Behind the tapestry. Go.”

I saw it then, a heavy hanging to the left of the altar, covering a pointed arch. I did not argue. I moved as the second brute shoved his wounded compatriot aside and drew a short, ugly blade.

I reached the tapestry, tore it aside. A small, lead-lined window, barely more than an arrow slit. It was not meant to open. I drove my elbow through it, the glass shattering with a sound like ice breaking on a river. I used the tapestry to clear the shards, hauled myself up into the narrow stone opening. I twisted, scraping through, and fell onto wet grass on the other side. I was in a walled garden, the rear of the chapel.

From inside, a choked cry, then the sound of a body hitting stone. Then silence.

A moment later, Elsa slipped through the broken window, landing cat-light beside me. There was a smear of blood across her cheek, not her own. Her eyes were bright, fierce.

“Ulrich?” I gasped.

“Leading the rest on a merry chase toward the river. He is a good rock in a stream. They break against him.” She looked at me, at the muck and the blood from the glass on my hands. “The seed?”

“Planted.”

A thin smile touched her lips. “Then the garden is poisoned. Good.”

We moved through the garden, over a low wall, into the labyrinth of the city. For a moment, we were free. The chase had been survived. The resolve tested, and found, if not strong, at least stubborn. The reward—the mission—was intact. The pamphlets were still hidden. Karlstadt had his weapon. We could slip away, back to Wittenberg, back to the role of distributors, smugglers in the night.

It was as that thought formed, a fragile bubble of hope, that the final, desperate attack came.

It did not come from men.

We were in a narrow close between high warehouses, heading for a postern gate Ulrich had described. The drizzle had stopped, leaving the world slick and gleaming. The sound was first: a deep, resonant thrum that seemed to come from the stones themselves. Then the smell: not dung, not rot, but the arid, terrifying scent of smoke on a grand scale.

We rounded a corner and saw it.

The building was a printer’s shop. I knew it by the sign: a press and a book. It was not ours. It was just a shop. Now, its lower windows bloomed with orange light. Flames licked up the timber facade, hungry and swift. The thrum was the sound of the fire eating the air.

And before it, standing in the street with a small group of black-clad figures, was Johann Tetzel.

He was not as I had imagined. He was not a giant of corruption. He was a man of average height, gone slightly to fat, with a face that might have been jovial if it were not set in lines of absolute, righteous fury. He held a burning torch in one hand. His other hand pointed at the inferno he had created.

“See!” his voice boomed, a practiced, sermonizing roar that cut through the crackle of the flames. “See the fate of lies! Of heresy! This den of poison is purged! Let it be a lesson to all who traffic in the devil’s script!”

It was a spectacle. A sermon written in fire. A crowd was gathering, drawn by the light and the drama. City guards stood at the edges, hesitant. Who were they to challenge a Dominican friar on a crusade?

He had not found our cache. He had not found us. So he had chosen another target. A random printer. A message. I will burn this city to the ground to burn your words.

The shop’s owner, a thin man in a singed apron, was on his knees in the mud, weeping. His life, his livelihood, turning to ash and sparks that rose to meet the dawn sky.

Tetzel turned, and his eyes swept the crowd. They passed over me, a filthy, nondescript figure in a cloak. They moved on. Then they snapped back. Something in my stance, my watchfulness, caught his hunter’s sense. Our eyes met across the heat-shimmered street.

He did not know me. But he knew what I was. I saw the recognition in his face—the same recognition I had felt in the chapel. An opposing force. A spiritual enemy.

He leaned to the man beside him, the clean-faced clerk from the fountain, who had somehow escaped the chapel. He pointed. Not at me. At Elsa, who stood half a step behind me.

The clerk nodded. He gestured. Two of the black-clad figures, big men with the look of off-duty soldiers, began to move around the edge of the crowd, toward us.

We could run. We could melt away. The gate was close. The chase would be hard, but we had evaded harder. The reward—the survival of the pamphlets, the completion of the delivery—was within our grasp. We could take it and go.

I looked at the weeping printer. I looked at the fire, Tetzel’s false purgatory. I felt the single remaining pamphlet in my tunic, the one I had kept as a last resort. The coal was now a white-hot brand.

The spiritual seeking had asked: what is truth? The living of it asked: what is truth worth?

Luther’s warning was a bell in my skull. A battlefield. My father’s quiet desk was a ghost. The choice under the Wittenberg gate was the foundation of my soul.

I made the critical choice.

I stepped forward, out of the shadow of the alley, into the full, flickering light of Tetzel’s fire.

I did not run. I did not hide.

I reached into my tunic, pulled out the last pamphlet. I held it high, so the gathering crowd could see the paper, the neat, heretical lines of type.

“You burn one house, Friar!” I shouted, and my voice did not sound like my own. It was raw, stripped, loud enough to turn heads. “But the words are already loose! They are in your university! They are in the hands of your rival! You cannot burn them all! Disputatio pro declaratione virtutis indulgentiarum! Read it and see the truth you try to hide with flame!”

For a second, there was only the sound of the fire. Tetzel’s face went from fury to a kind of incredulous, apoplectic triumph. I had given myself to him. Publicly. Defiantly.

The two men changed course, coming straight for me.

Elsa’s hand gripped my arm. “Fool,” she breathed, but there was no anger in it. Only a stark understanding.

I shook her off. “Go. Tell Ulrich. The seed is sown. The bait is taken. Protect the rest.”

I looked at her, and in that look, I sacrificed the reward—my safety, my freedom, the successful, quiet completion of the task. I traded it for a greater good: a public confrontation. A story that would spread faster than any pamphlet. A student stood against Tetzel’s fire. Let that be the rumor. Let that be the spark.

She hesitated for only a heartbeat. Then she was gone, a shadow dissolving into deeper shadow.

The two men reached me. They did not ask questions. One drove a fist into my stomach. The air left me in a whoosh, and I folded over the pamphlet, clutching it to my gut. They hauled me upright, dragged me through the mud toward the burning light, toward Tetzel.

The crowd parted. Their faces were a blur of fear, curiosity, awe. I was a spectacle now, too. Part of the sermon.

They threw me at Tetzel’s feet. I landed hard, the pamphlet slipping from my grasp onto the wet cobbles. The friar looked down at me, then at the paper. He did not pick it up. He ground his heel onto it, twisting, grinding it into the muck and filth.

“Another rat from the nest,” he announced to the crowd. “Carrying the pestilence. He will be cleansed. He will be shown the error of his ways. And he will tell us where the rest of his filth is hidden.”

He leaned down, his face close to mine. I could smell the incense on his robes, the oil in his hair, the sourness of his breath. His eyes were not empty like the clerk’s. They were full—full of a terrifying, absolute certainty.

“You have chosen to be a weapon, boy,” he whispered, so only I could hear. “Now you will learn what happens to weapons that challenge the Church. They are melted down. In the hottest of fires.”

The guards finally stepped forward. Tetzel waved a dismissive hand. “He is mine. A spiritual matter. Take him to the Pleissenburg. The Dominican cells there.”

As they hauled me to my feet, my arms wrenched behind my back, I looked past him. I looked at the fire. I looked at the crowd. I had sought God in silence and scripture. I had found Him here, in the roar of a false fire and the terrified eyes of the crowd. He was not in the blessing. He was in the warning. He was in the choice.

They dragged me away. I did not struggle. I had made my choice. The hook was set. I was in the dragon’s mouth.

As I was pulled from the square, I saw a massive figure standing in the shadows of a far alley. Ulrich. He did not move to intervene. He watched, his face a stone mask. He saw the sacrifice. He would protect the greater good.

The last thing I saw was the printer’s shop, its roof collapsing in a fountain of sparks, and Tetzel, silhouetted against the hell-glow, already turning to address his captured audience, a new chapter in his sermon of fear.

They took me into the cold stone of the Pleissenburg, down steps that smelled of damp and despair. The sound of the city faded, replaced by the drip of water, the clang of a distant door. They threw me into a cell no larger than a grave, with a slit of a window high up, showing a thin slice of dawn the color of a fresh wound.

The spiritual seeking was over. The living of it was here, in the dark.

I sat on the straw, the stench of the midden and the smoke still on me. My body ached. My heart was a frantic bird against my ribs. But beneath the fear, a terrible, solid calm was forming.

I had provoked the dragon. Now I was in its belly. The pamphlet was mud under Tetzel’s heel, but the words were in Karlstadt’s head. The seed was sown. The confrontation had begun.

In the darkness of the cell, I finally understood the full consequence of the choice under the gate. It was not a single moment. It was a road, paved with your own breaking, leading to a place where you either became the truth you sought, or you were erased by the lies that opposed it.

Somewhere, Elsa was moving through the city like a rumour. Somewhere, Ulrich was a gathering storm. Somewhere, Luther waited in Wittenberg, his warning now a lived reality.

And somewhere to the east, my father sat at his quiet desk, believing his son was safe.

The hook was set. I was the bait. And as I heard the heavy tread of boots approaching my cell door, the turn of a key in a massive lock, I knew the dragon had begun to swallow.

The next move was his. And mine would have to be made from inside the beast.

Chapter 9

CHAPTER NINE

The key turned in the lock with a sound like a bone breaking. The door groaned open, not with the fury of a raid, but with the slow, oiled finality of a ritual. Two guards stood there, their faces flat and empty in the guttering torchlight from the corridor. They did not speak. They merely waited.

The calm that had formed in me during the night was a cold stone in my gut. I stood, my muscles screaming from the damp and the hard floor, and walked toward them. The straw clung to my wet hose. I did not brush it off. Let them see the filth. Let it be part of the testimony.

They led me not up toward the light and sound of the castle proper, but deeper down. The air grew colder, damper, the smell of despair thickening into something more profound: the scent of stone that had never seen the sun, of time measured only in drips from the ceiling. This was not a place for prisoners. This was a place for forgetting.

We stopped before an iron-bound door. One guard produced another key. The other looked at me, his eyes reflecting the torch flame like a dumb animal’s. “He waits,” he grunted, the first words spoken since my arrest.

The door swung inward onto darkness. Not the simple dark of a windowless room, but a profound, velvety black that seemed to swallow the torchlight whole. A hand—not a guard’s, its touch was dry and papery—closed around my upper arm and pulled me inside. The door shut behind me, plunging us into absolute night. The sound of the locking mechanism was a soft, precise click. A scholar’s sound.

“Sit, Brother Thomas.”

The voice was not Tetzel’s. It was higher, thinner, a wire drawn taut. It came from directly ahead. I did not move. The hand on my arm released me.

“I said, sit.”

A spark flared, illuminating for an instant a long, narrow table, a skeletal hand holding a tinderbox, and the sharp, pale planes of a face I did not know. The spark touched the wick of a single candle. The flame grew, pushing the darkness back to the corners of the room, which seemed to drink it hungrily.

The man was gaunt, wrapped in a Dominican’s black robe, but it hung on him as on a pole. His hair was a grey fringe around a tonsure so precise it looked surgical. His eyes were the color of a winter puddle, reflecting the candle flame but generating no light of their own. He gestured to a simple stool on my side of the table. On his side was a high-backed chair. Between us, on the table’s scarred surface, lay two objects: my pamphlet, mud-stained and crumpled, and a blank sheet of pristine vellum, a quill, and a small pot of ink.

“I am Inquisitor Silvestro,” he said, seating himself. He did not blink. “You will sit.”

I sat. The stool was uneven. It forced me to lean slightly, off-balance.

“This,” he said, laying a finger on the pamphlet without looking at it, “is a pustule. A symptom. Tetzel deals in symptoms. He lances them with fire and spectacle. Necessary work, but crude.” His winter eyes lifted to mine. “I am a physician of the soul. I seek the humoral imbalance. The root of the heresy.”

He spoke Latin, the clean, sharp Latin of the universities, not the church-yard Latin of the priests. This was a different kind of dragon. One that did not roar, but dissected.

“You are not from Leipzig,” I said. My voice was rough with disuse.

“I am from where error is,” he replied. “Your little performance at the printing quarter was… illustrative. The passionate defense. The willing martyrdom. The comrade left to burn. A familiar drama.” He tilted his head. “But you are no simple zealot. Your text, though vile, is not the ranting of a lunatic. It is structured. It argues. It uses their own tools. That makes you more dangerous than a hundred flagellants. You are a translator of poison.”

He picked up the pamphlet, smoothed a page with a terrifying tenderness. “You speak of the ‘inner certainty.’ Of the ‘priesthood of all believers.’ You strip the Church of its authority, its mediation, its very flesh. You leave only a naked soul and a distant God. A terrifying, empty universe. That is the true heresy. Not the criticism of indulgences, but the architecture of loneliness you build in its place.”

He set it down. “Why?”

It was not a question asked in anger, but in clinical curiosity. A botanist examining a novel weed.

“It is the truth,” I said, the words ash in my mouth.

“Truth,” he repeated, tasting the word. “A singular noun. You presume there is only one. The Church is a living body, Brother Thomas. It digests, it grows, it interprets. Its truth is manifold, evolving, protected. Your ‘truth’ is a corpse—static, brittle, and infectious.”

He pushed the vellum, quill, and ink toward me. The movement was fluid, practiced. “You will write a recantation. Not of the pamphlet. That is the symptom. You will recant the principle. You will affirm, in your own hand, with your own reasoning, the necessity of Mother Church as the sole conduit of grace, the indispensable interpreter of Scripture, the final authority on earth. You will anatomize your own error and in so doing, cleanse it.”

I stared at the blank skin of the vellum. It glowed in the candlelight. “And if I do not?”

He did not smile. “Then we will continue our discussion. I have all the tools required to help you find the right words. Pain, as the poets tell us, is a great clarifier. But I am not a savage. I would prefer your mind to surrender, not just your body. A broken body can still house a rebellious spirit. A persuaded mind is a soul at peace.”

The cold stone of my calm began to sweat. This was the test. Not the fire, not the mob, not the dungeon’s gloom. This was the clean, well-lit room where faith was taken apart like a faulty clock. Tetzel wanted to burn the book. Silvestro wanted to rewrite it, with my own hand.

“I have nothing to write,” I said.

“Then we begin,” he said, and blew out the candle.

The darkness was his instrument. Time dissolved in it. He would speak, his voice disembodied, from one corner, then another.

“Consider the sun,” the voice would come, from behind my left ear. “You feel its warmth. You see by its light. But could your eye perceive it directly? It would burn you to blindness. So it is with God. The Church is the atmosphere that diffuses His radiance, makes it bearable, livable. Your ‘inner certainty’ is the vanity of a blind man who denies the sun exists because he feels only the cold.”

Silence. The drip of water. The rustle of his robe, moving.

“Your Luther,” the voice said, now from the right. “He makes every man his own pope. What happens when one man’s ‘inner certainty’ tells him to lie, to steal, to kill? Where is the check? Your world is a world of a million petty tyrants, each a god in his own skull. Is that freedom? Or is it hell?”

He never raised his voice. He never threatened. He simply dissected, layer by layer, the foundations of everything I had come to believe. He found the fears I had buried—the terror of being truly alone before God, the awful weight of personal responsibility—and he named them. He called them the logical end of my path.

Hours passed. Or days. My bladder burned. My throat was parched. He would occasionally give me water, a cup pressed into my hands in the dark. A small kindness that felt like the greatest cruelty.

Then, the questions turned personal.

“The printer, Karlstadt. You led him to the fire. Your words lit the kindling. Do you feel the heat of it on your face, even here?”

I clenched my jaw.

“The girl, Elsa. A curious soul. Vulnerable. How safe will her ‘inner certainty’ be when the world turns on her for following you? You offer her a sword, but no armor.”

My breath hitched. He heard it. He was silent for a long time, letting the thought fester.

“And your father,” the voice finally came, soft, almost sorrowful, from directly in front of me. “The good doctor. He believes you are studying canon law. He dreams of a son in a cathedral chapter, a comfort to his old age. You are building his heart a prison, stone by stone. Every word you write is a bar in his cell. That is the fruit of your truth. Solitude. Betrayal. Pain. For everyone who touches you.”

The stone of my calm shattered. It was not a dramatic breaking, but a quiet collapse into dust. He was right. He was right. My quest had left a trail of wreckage. Karlstadt’s shop was ash. Elsa was adrift. My father’s peace was a lie. I had traded the living, breathing, flawed body of the Church for a ghost of an idea, and the ghost offered no comfort, no community, no absolution. Only the terrible, echoing courtroom of the self.

A sob wrenched itself from my chest. It was the sound of utter defeat.

The candle flared to life. Silvestro was seated again, his face a mask of calm understanding. He saw the tears on my face, the trembling of my hands. He saw the hollowed-out vessel.

He pushed the vellum forward once more. The blank page was no longer an accusation. It was an offer. A way home. A way to stop the damage. To recant was not just to save my skin. It was to repair the world I had broken.

My hand, moving as if of its own will, reached for the quill.

My fingers closed around the shaft of the feather. It was cool, smooth. The reality of it—the physical act of dipping it in the black ink, of bringing it to the pure, expectant vellum—was a cliff’s edge. On one side, an end to the struggle. An end to being the splinter in the flesh of everyone I loved. A return to the known world, even if it was a world of beautiful, necessary lies.

Silvestro watched, his winter eyes patient. He had won. He knew it. This was the moment of conversion, quieter than any sermon, more final than any fire.

I looked down at the blankness. And in that whiteness, I did not see absolution.

I saw the rain on the pilgrim road, the cold finger tracing my spine. I saw the words on the cathedral door, not as doctrine, but as a life lived: the just shall live by faith. Not by the permission of institutions. Not by the recantation of fear. By faith. A lived faith. A bloody, muddy, breaking-road faith.

Karlstadt’s face, lit by press-fire, not by terror, but by a fierce, defiant joy. It is worth it.

Elsa’s voice in the stable loft, not fragile, but fierce. I am not a leaf.

Ulrich’s stone mask in the alley, watching me be taken. Protecting not me, but the thing itself. The spark.

My father at his desk, yes. But not dreaming of a canon. Teaching me to read a wound, to trace a fever to its source. He was a physician of bodies. He had taught me to seek the cause.

Silvestro’s truth was a perfect, closed system. A clockwork universe of authority and grace. But it had no room for Karlstadt’s joy. It had no name for Elsa’s courage. It called Ulrich’s sacrifice a political calculation. It would diagnose my father’s love as a sentimental obstruction.

My seeking had not been for a new set of answers to replace the old. It had been for a way to live in the question. To stand in the terrible, glorious, uncertain space between God and man, without a robe, without a title, without a guarantee. To have only the evidence of a heart being remade.

The quill trembled. But not with weakness now. With a terrible, focused energy.

I dipped it into the inkpot. I brought it to the vellum. Silvestro leaned forward, a faint, approving crease at the corner of his mouth.

I did not write a word of recantation.

I drew a line. A single, bold, black, horizontal line across the center of the page. Then I dropped the quill. It rolled, spattering tiny drops of ink like black stars across the pristine surface.

Silvestro’s breath froze. The approving crease vanished.

“What is this?” His voice was still thin, but the wire was vibrating.

“That,” I said, my own voice clear for the first time, “is the ground. The level place. The common earth upon which we all stand—pope, prince, printer, and pauper. Before God, there is no high chair, no low stool. There is only the line. And we are all on it.”

It was not Luther’s theology. It was not a argument from scripture. It was the picture that had formed in the dark of my cell, in the deeper dark of his examination. It was the sum of my seeking. A foundational, spiritual truth, reduced to an image a ploughman could understand.

He stared at the defaced vellum as if it were a severed limb. His clinical detachment cracked. “Fool. You have signed your own condemnation. You have chosen oblivion.”

“I have chosen the line,” I said. “You can have me burned on it. But you cannot un-draw it. It is in your head now. And it is the truth.”

He rose so swiftly his chair shrieked on the stone. For the first time, I saw passion in him—a cold, furious rage. He was not offended by heresy. He was offended by mess. By the irrational, indelible mark. I had refused to play the game of words, and in doing so, I had broken his most precious tool: reason.

He snatched the candle. “Then you will learn the price of your doodle in the dark.”

But the lesson was over. I had learned it. The victory was not in escaping the fire. It was in choosing what would be burned. They could burn my body. They could not burn the line. It existed now. In me.

The door burst open.

Not with the slow, oiled motion of before, but with a splintering crash. Torchlight flooded the room, and with it came the smell of the world—of smoke, night air, and horse sweat.

Ulrich filled the doorway. His face was not a stone mask now. It was a living cliff of fury, streaked with soot and blood that was not his own. In one hand he held a massive iron key, bent. In the other, a guard’s short sword, dripping.

Behind him, the corridor was a chaos of shouts and running boots.

Silvestro stumbled back, the candle flame guttering in the sudden draft. “What is the meaning of this? This is the—”

“The Pleissenburg is burning,” Ulrich growled, his voice the sound of the foundation stones themselves grinding. “Tetzel’s zealots and the city’s poor are at each other’s throats. Your orderly world is coming apart at the seams, Inquisitor. Leipzig is eating itself.”

He looked at me, saw the ink on my fingers, the defiant line on the vellum. Something like a grim smile touched his eyes. “Time to go, Brother Thomas. The dragon is choking on its own bile.”

Silvestro pointed a bony finger at me, his composure in ashes. “That man is a condemned heretic! You cannot—”

“I am not taking him,” Ulrich said, stepping into the room. “I am releasing him. The choice of where to go is his.” He looked at me. “The back gates are unguarded. The streets are chaos. You can vanish. To Wittenberg, to the ends of the earth.” He then turned his gaze back to Silvestro, a predator’s focus. “Or you can stay. And bear witness to what comes next.”

It was the final, purified test. Not between recantation and martyrdom, but between flight and fulfillment. The spiritual seeking was over. The living of it was here. In the choice.

I stood. My legs held. I walked past Silvestro, who shrank back as if from a leper. I stopped beside Ulrich at the shattered door.

I looked back at the Inquisitor, cringing in his circle of guttering candlelight, the defaced vellum between us like a declaration of war.

“The line, Father,” I said. “Remember it.”

Then I turned to Ulrich. “Not the back gates.”

He raised a bushy eyebrow.

“Take me to the front. To the square.”

A slow, fierce understanding dawned on his face. He gave a single, sharp nod. “Then we go through the fire.”

We went up through the belly of the beast, and the beast was in its death throes. The Pleissenburg was a hive of panic. Shouts echoed down stone corridors. Men ran both toward the fighting and away from it. Ulrich moved with a terrifying purpose, a force of nature in a confined space. We encountered two guards; a flash of his stolen sword, a crash of his fist, and they were down. He did not kill if he could help it. He simply removed obstacles.

The sounds from outside grew—not just shouts, but the roar of a crowd, the crackle of flame, the distant, terrible crash of breaking masonry. The sweet-rot stench of the city was now overwhelmed by the acrid pall of a major fire.

We emerged not into the main courtyard, but onto a high parapet overlooking the market square. The scene below was a painting of hell, animated.

The printer’s quarter was a bonfire, painting the underside of the low, smoky sky in pulsing oranges and reds. But the fire had jumped. A nearby granary was ablaze, tongues of fire licking at the dawn sky. The square itself was a churning mass of humanity. Tetzel’s guards, in their distinctive livery, had formed a bristling knot around the raised preaching platform, where the indulgence preacher himself stood, not preaching now, but screaming orders, his face a grotesque mask of rage and soot. Arrayed against them was a mob—not just students, but tradesmen, beggars, women with iron pots and broom handles. And between them, lashing both sides, were the city watch, trying and failing to impose order.

This was the fruit of Tetzel’s sermon of fear. This was the consequence of a Church that sold salvation and threatened hell. It had created not piety, but a pressure cooker of resentment. The spark of my pamphlet, of Karlstadt’s press, had simply found the powder.

“There is your victory,” Ulrich shouted over the din, his hand on my shoulder. “Chaos. Is this the ‘priesthood of all believers’?”

“No,” I shouted back, the heat of the fires washing over my face. “This is the death rattle of the old priesthood!”

He studied me, his eyes searching for doubt. He saw none. The calm was back, but it was no longer cold stone. It was the calm of the line. The level ground. This chaos was not my truth. It was the violent expulsion of a lie.

“What would you have me do?” I asked him.

“You started this. Finish it.”

He pointed down, not to the mob, but to the space between the mob and the guards. A no-man’s-land of shattered stalls and trampled goods. “They need a third thing. Not Tetzel’s tyranny. Not the mob’s rage. Something else. Or this city will burn to ash, and with it, any hope for your ‘new way.’”

He was right. The spiritual victory in the cell was meaningless if it only birthed a physical hell. The truth had to be lived here, in the mud and the blood and the fire, or it was no truth at all.

I saw the way down—a narrow servant’s stair, half-hidden, leading to a postern gate that opened near the edge of the square.

I looked at Ulrich. “And you?”

“I will be the storm,” he said simply. “Go. Your pulpit awaits.”

I took the stairs two at a time, my heart hammering not with fear, but with a terrible, focused urgency. The postern gate was unbarred, the guard gone to the fray. I pushed it open and stepped into the cacophony of the square.

The heat was immense. The air tasted of cinders and fury. I was a ghost in my filthy clothes, unnoticed, moving along the edge of the chaos. I had no plan. No words prepared. Only the line. The level ground.

I saw a cart, overturned, its wheel broken. I climbed onto its side. It gave me a foot of height. Nothing more.

I drew a breath, filling my lungs with smoke and destiny, and I shouted.

It was not a mighty roar. It was the voice of a student, ragged from the dungeon, and it was swallowed by the din. No one heard. No one turned.

I was a whisper in a hurricane.

Then, a stone flew from the mob, striking a guardsman in the helm. He fell. His comrades roared, lowering their pikes. The mob surged forward with a collective scream. The moment of massacre was here.

I did the only thing I could think of. I jumped down from the cart, into the no-man’s-land. I walked, not toward the mob, not toward the guards, but directly into the empty, dangerous space between them.

I turned my back on the bristling pikes. I faced the oncoming tide of rage. I spread my empty hands, showing I held no stone, no weapon. I was just a man, standing on the line.

A few in the front saw me. They hesitated, confused. “Out of the way, fool!” one roared, a big cooper with a hammer.

I did not move. I looked past him, into the faces of the crowd. I saw Karlstadt there, his arm bandaged, his eyes wild. I saw faces I recognized from the printing quarter, from the taverns, twisted by hate and fear.

“Stop!” I cried, my voice finding a strange, carrying strength from a place I did not know I possessed.

The cooper lunged, grabbing my sodden tunic. “You’re with them!”

“I am with you!” I shouted, gripping his wrist. “And burning Leipzig will not buy you heaven! It will only buy you a graveyard!”

He shook me, but I held on. My eyes locked with his. “He sells fear! Do you buy it? Or do you live without it?”

Something in the directness, in the sheer insanity of standing alone between two charging armies, gave them pause. The front of the mob slowed, piling into itself. The guards, seeing the hesitation, held their charge.

In that fragile, silent second, I turned. I walked now toward the guards, toward Tetzel on his platform. The guardsmen raised their pikes, aiming at my chest.

I kept walking. “You are sworn to keep the peace!” I shouted up at them. “This is not peace! This is your master’s harvest! Look at it!”

I was ten paces from the spear points. Five.

“Shoot him!” Tetzel screamed from the platform, his voice shrill. “He is the heretic! He caused this!”

A crossbowman leveled his weapon. His hand was shaking.

I stopped. I looked at him, a young man, his face pale under his helm. “The line,” I said, quietly now, but the words carried in the sudden, watchful silence. “We are all on it. Even him.” I pointed at Tetzel. “Even you. God sees us all on the same ground. Put down the weapon that pretends otherwise.”

The crossbowman’s eyes flicked from me, to the mob, to Tetzel, who was now shrieking incoherently. He did not lower his bow, but his finger came off the trigger.

A new sound cut through the square—not a shout, but a deep, resonant clang. Then another. And another.

The bell of the Thomaskirche. Tolling not for service, but for alarm. For a city in peril.

Its great, mournful voice rolled over the square, a sound of authority older than Tetzel, older than the mob. It was the voice of the ordinary world, crying enough.

And from the mouth of an alley opposite the guards’ formation, a new group emerged. Not rioters. Burghers. Merchants in fine wool, their faces set with pragmatic anger. At their head was a tall, grey-bearded man in the chain of the city council. They carried no weapons, but their presence was a wall.

“Enough!” the grey-bearded man boomed, his voice accustomed to command. “Tetzel! You will order your men to stand down. You will come with us. Your preaching in Leipzig is concluded.”

Tetzel sputtered, “You have no authority over the Church’s business—”

“I have authority over Leipzig’s streets!” the councilman thundered. “And they run with blood and fire because of you. Stand down, or we will drag you down.”

The guard captain looked from his raving master to the solid wall of the city fathers, to the paused mob, to me, still standing between them all. He made a sharp gesture. The pikes lifted. The formation parted.

The spell was broken. The immediate violence was bled away, replaced by a tense, shuddering stalemate.

I stood alone in the center, the bell’s toll washing over me. I had not won with a brilliant argument. I had not defeated Tetzel with logic. I had simply stood on the line. And for a moment, just a moment, everyone had seen it.

The cooper released my tunic. He took a step back, looking at his hammer as if he didn’t know how it got there. Karlstadt pushed through to the front, his eyes on me, wide with something that was not fear.

From the parapet high above, I felt a gaze. Ulrich. Watching.

Tetzel was being led away by the burghers, still protesting, but his power was gone, sucked out by the chaos he had fed. The mob began to disperse, the fight gone out of them, turning instead to the desperate work of fighting the fires that were now the true enemy.

The ordinary world was cleansing itself. Not with a pronouncement from Rome, but with the pragmatic, grimy work of survival. The spiritual poison was being expelled in the sweat and smoke.

I turned, my legs weak, and walked back toward the overturned cart. My work was done. The confrontation was over. I had embodied the new self—not a destroyer of the old world, but a witness to the new ground upon which it must be rebuilt.

As I reached the edge of the square, a small, soot-streaked figure detached from the shadow of a leaning house and fell into step beside me. Elsa. She said nothing. She simply took my hand, her grip fierce and sure. She had been there. She had seen it all.

We melted into the smoky alleyways, leaving the square to its bells and its fires. The dawn was fully come now, a dirty, beautiful grey, the color of ashes and new beginnings.

We walked toward the eastern gate, toward the road that led to Wittenberg. Toward Luther. Toward the next chapter of the living truth.

But as we passed a boarded-up tavern, its sign creaking in the morning wind, a figure stepped from a doorway. Not Ulrich. A man I had never seen before, dressed in the travel-stained clothes of a minor noble or a wealthy merchant. He had a sharp, intelligent face and eyes that missed nothing.

He bowed his head slightly, not in reverence, but in assessment. “Thomas,” he said. His voice was cultured, calm. “A remarkable performance. From the dungeon to the square in one night. You have a talent for the dramatic.”

I stopped, pulling Elsa slightly behind me. “Who are you?”

“A friend,” he said, smiling a thin smile. “Or perhaps a colleague. My master has been watching your progress with great interest. He believes you and Doctor Luther may be… missing a piece of the puzzle.”

“What puzzle?” “The political puzzle,” the man said. “Spiritual truth is a lovely thing. But to change the world, it needs a prince’s protection. Or an empire’s leverage.”

He stepped closer, lowering his voice. “My master is in Wittenberg. He wishes to meet with you. Before you see the good doctor. He has a proposal. One that could ensure your ‘line’ is not drawn in sand, but carved into the foundations of states.”

He pressed a small, sealed letter into my hand. The wax seal was unfamiliar—a stylized, coiled serpent.

“Consider it,” the man said. “The road you are on is dangerous. It helps to have powerful friends. Even… especially… the kind who work in shadows.”

He bowed again, turned, and was gone, swallowed by the smoke.

I stood holding the letter, the serpent seal warm in my palm. Elsa looked from it to my face, her own full of silent question.

The dragon of Rome was choking. But as I looked at the clever, coiled serpent in the wax, I understood.

The beast had many heads. And the next one was already waiting for me, not with a hammer, but with a promise.

Chapter 10

CHAPTER TEN

The mud of Wittenberg was the same. That was the first, foolish thought that struck me as we passed under the shadow of the Elster Gate. The same churned, shit-flecked slurry that had sucked at my boots the day I left. The same beggars huddled in the same alcoves, the same fishwives shouting the same prices in voices like tearing rust. The world had turned on its axis, heaven itself had been cracked open with a hammer-blow of truth, and yet the mud remained. It was a mockery. Or a lesson. I was too tired to know which.

Elsa walked beside me, her hand a small, firm pressure on my arm. Not for support, but for anchoring. Her eyes, wide and dark, moved over the familiar streets as if reading a text in a foreign tongue. She saw the prison in the ordinary. The leering gargoyle on the guildhall became a spy; the shutters closing on an upper story were eyes squeezing shut against a blasphemous sight. She had crossed her own river, and there was no returning to the other bank.

Ulrich walked a pace ahead, his bulk carving a path through the press. He said nothing, but his shoulders were a wall. He had not asked about the man with the serpent seal. He had only watched me break it, read the few precise lines within, and tuck the parchment inside my tunic. His silence was a question heavier than any words.

The letter was an address. A house on the Collegienstrasse, near the university. After Vespers, it said. No name. Just the serpent, and a time.

“You feel that, Brother Thomas?” Ulrich grunted, not turning his head.

“The mud?”

“The quiet.”

I listened. Beneath the hawkers’ cries and the rumble of carts, there was a new note. A held breath. The sermons from the Castle Church had blown through this place like a gale, scattering the old leaves of indulgence and fear. But gales leave silence in their wake. Men stood in clumps, talking in low tones, their eyes darting. A priest scurried past, head down, his robes seeming too large for him. The air was thick with a spent energy, and the tension of what must come next. It was the quiet of a battlefield after the first volley, before the charge.

“They are waiting,” I said.

“Aye,” Ulrich said. “For him. And now, perhaps, for you.”

My father’s house smelled of yeast and old wood and the ghost of my mother’s lavender. It was a smell that had once meant the whole and certain world. Standing in the low-ceilinged main room, with Elsa shivering slightly by the cold hearth, it felt like the memory of a stranger’s life.

My sister, Katherina, emerged from the bake-room, wiping floury hands on her apron. She saw Ulrich first, and her face arranged itself into polite wariness for the large, unknown monk. Then her gaze found me.

It did not light with joy. It fractured.

Her eyes travelled over me—the ragged, travel-stained clothes that were not my own, the gauntness of my face, the new scar on my brow from the stone in Torgau. They went to Elsa, to her shorn hair and defiant, exhausted posture. Katherina’s hands, still white with dough, rose slowly to her mouth.

“Thomas?”

It was a whisper, as if speaking the name too loud might confirm a nightmare.

“I am home, Kat,” I said. The words were ash.

She did not run to me. She took a single step, then stopped, her eyes searching mine. She was looking for her brother. The boy who worried over ledger books, who jumped at his own shadow, who believed the world was ordered and God’s favour could be earned like a guild fee. That boy was not here. In his place was this burnt-out thing, standing in a pool of melted snow and mud, with a quiet violence in his stillness and a heretic girl at his side.

“Your hair,” she finally said, her voice trembling. “Your beautiful hair.”

I had forgotten. My head, shorn in the dungeon, was covered only in coarse, dark bristle. A penitent’s scalp. A rebel’s.

“It was the price of a view,” I said.

She did not understand. She shook her head, and the tears came then, not of welcome but of loss. “We heard… stories. That you were taken. That you spoke against the Cardinal. They said you were to be burned.” Her voice hitched. “Father… he has not left his workshop for three days. He prays. He does not eat.”

“Where is he?”

She gestured weakly toward the back. I moved past her, my wet boots heavy on the floorboards. Elsa made to follow, but Ulrich put a gentle hand on her shoulder. “Give them the room,” he murmured.

My father’s workshop was a cave of ordered chaos. Curls of sweet-smelling oak and ash littered the floor. Half-formed lute bodies hung like strange fruit from the rafters. He was at his bench, but he was not working. He sat on his stool, his broad, calloused hands resting on his knees, staring at a finished lute neck, its fretboard inlaid with mother-of-pearl. A tiny, perfect rose.

He did not look up as I entered. The rain tapped a feeble rhythm on the small window.

“Father.”

His shoulders tightened. Slowly, he turned. His face, always so stern and certain, was a landscape of erosion. Deep lines of fear had been cut beside his mouth. His eyes, the same shade as my own, held not anger, but a profound, weary confusion.

“They told me my son was dead,” he said. His voice was the rasp of a saw on hard wood. “Then they told me he was a devil. Then they told me he was a saint.” He blinked, slowly. “Which is it, Thomas? What have you brought home?”

“The truth,” I said, and the word, which had felt like a fire in my chest in the square, now felt inadequate. A child’s word.

“Truth.” He said it as if tasting a strange spice. “And what is the cost of this truth? Your life? Your soul? Our standing? Master Haas has already withdrawn his daughter’s betrothal offer. He says our family is… spiritually tainted.” A flicker of the old man then, pride wounded by a petty slight. But it died quickly. “They say you stood before a prince of the Church and called him a liar. They say you have thrown your lot in with the Augustinian. That you seek to tear down Christ’s own house.”

“I seek to open the windows,” I said, stepping closer. The smell of him—sawdust, glue, honest sweat—was an ache in my throat. “The house is rotten, Father. It is built on money and fear. It locks away the scripture and sells the key. It tells us God is a merchant, and our prayers are coins. It is wrong.”

He flinched as if I’d struck him. “It is Holy Mother Church.”

“It is a dragon,” I said, the image from the square rising up, vivid and choking. “And it is eating the faithful alive.”

Silence fell, thick with the unsaid. He looked at my hands, empty of the tools he’d taught me to cherish. He looked at my face, empty of the fear he’d known.

“Who is the girl?” he asked finally.

“Her name is Elsa. They killed her father for reading Luther’s New Testament. She has no one.”

“And you are someone now? A protector of orphans?” There was no malice in it, only a vast bewilderment. “You left here a boy, afraid of his own thoughts. You return a… a knight. For a cause I do not know. You speak in metaphors of beasts and windows. This is not my son.”

“He is gone,” I said, and the admission was a relief. “The boy who lived in fear of hellfire and his father’s disapproval died in the archbishop’s dungeon. What walked out is something else.”

He held my gaze for a long moment, and I saw the moment he accepted it. His eyes grew damp, not with tears, but with a kind of resigned clarity. He was a craftsman. He recognized a piece that had been fundamentally reshaped, remade for a purpose he could not discern.

“This truth,” he said softly, turning back to the lute neck. “This living truth you walk toward. Does it bring peace?”

I thought of the square in Torgau, of the shared breath of the crowd, of the line in the dirt. I thought of the serpent in my tunic. “No,” I said. “It brings a sword.”

He nodded, as if I had confirmed his deepest dread. “Then you cannot stay here, Thomas. You will bring that sword into my house. You will put your sister, and this girl you protect, in its path.” He picked up a small chisel, his grip familiar and tight. “You must go to him. To Luther. Let him be your father now. Let his cloister be your home.”

It was a dismissal. Not in rage, but in a terrible, loving pragmatism. He was cutting me away from the stock to save the rest. I felt the severing, clean and cold.

“I understand,” I said.

“I do not think you do,” he whispered, and began, with meticulous care, to scrape a sliver of excess pearl from the rose inlay. The work would continue. The world of wood and wire and perfect, silent song would endure. I was of another world now, a world of words and fire.

I left him there, carving beauty in the midst of the wreckage I had brought home.
