He was warning me off the pamphlet, off the direct line against Tetzel. He was a businessman. He saw the looming loss, the entanglement with power.

“I understand the economics, Master Fust,” I said. “But some debts are not financial. Some payments are demanded in a different coin.”

He studied me for a long moment, seeing, perhaps, the new hardness in my face, the shadows under my eyes that were not just from lack of sleep. “You have the look of a man who has seen the ledger’s bottom line, and found it written in red,” he said. “I will not ask. But remember: my investment is in the machine. In its product. Protect that. All else is sentiment.”

I left with a smaller, heavier purse of coin—payment for the delivered work. It was the fuel for my thin shield in Koblenz, for the chain, for the bread that kept us going. As I walked back, the oilcloth-wrapped calendar under my arm, I felt the two worlds grinding against each other. Fust’s world of product and profit. And my world, which had become one of vulnerable cords and threatened red doors.

I turned into the street that led to the print-shop. And I stopped.

The door was ajar.

Not wide. Just a hand’s breadth. But it was never ajar. Not with the chain inside. Not with Ulrich on watch.

The cold knot in my stomach became a glacier. I dropped the calendar sheets into the mud, my hand going to the small, sharp knife I now carried in my belt. I approached not like a man approaching home, but like a man approaching an ambush.

I pushed the door slowly. It swung inward with a low groan.

The first thing I saw was the chain. It lay in a heavy, coiled heap on the floor, the padlock still fastened. It had not been cut. It had been unlocked.

The second thing I saw was Ulrich. He was on his knees in the centre of the room, his head bowed. His cudgel lay broken beside him. From behind, he looked in prayer.

Then I saw the third thing.

Standing beside the press, one hand resting casually on the great screw, was a man. He was of average height, dressed in the worn but serviceable clothes of a journeyman carpenter or a farrier. His face was unremarkable, forgettable. But in his other hand, held loose and easy, was a tool. Not a knife. A pair of long, slender shears, the kind used for trimming metal or cutting thick wire. The blades were open, sharp and clean.

He looked at me, his expression one of mild interest. He did not speak. He just lifted the shears and, with a slow, deliberate snick, cut the air.

The sound was the hook. It was not in the shears, or in the open door, or in Ulrich’s bowed head. It was in the man’s other hand, the one resting on the screw. Tucked between his fingers was a small, familiar object. A twist of straw, tied in a particular, rustic knot.

The kind used to bind sheaves of wheat in the Moselle valley.

He had not come for the cord. He had come to show me he could. He had come to show me he had been to the source of the thread. The performance was over. The story had been rewritten.

And the next chapter would be written in the space between that silent snick and the straw knot from home.

Chapter 16

CHAPTER SIXTEEN

The snick of the shears was a sound that did not fade. It hung in the damp air of the room, a punctuation mark sharper than the rain’s. It severed the moment cleanly from the one before. The pilgrim road, the mud, the calendar pages bleeding ink in the muck outside—all of that was a story already told. This was a new page, and the man by the press was holding the quill.

Ulrich did not move. His bowed back was a mountain of defeat. The broken cudgel spoke of a struggle that had been brief, clinical, and utterly one-sided.

My own knife felt like a toy in my hand. A child’s stick against a woodsman’s axe. The man with the shears watched me recognize this. His mild interest did not change. He was a craftsman assessing material.

“You are Thomas,” he said. His voice was as unremarkable as his face, a middle note with no accent I could place. It was the voice of a man who could be from anywhere, and therefore from nowhere.

I said nothing. My eyes were on the twist of straw between his fingers on the screw. That knot was a village secret. Old Man Fischer taught it to me when I was six, his gnarled hands over mine. For the Moselle wheat, boy. It breathes. This man had held it. He had stood where I had stood. The thought was a violation more intimate than any blade.

“You have been asking questions,” he said. He didn’t move the shears. They were simply part of his hand, an extension of his will. “Tracing threads. A dangerous hobby for a printer’s devil.”

“What have you done to him?” My voice was a stranger’s, tight and high.

The man glanced at Ulrich as if noticing him for the first time. “Persuaded him to kneel. He is a stubborn root. The persuasion was… structural.” He nodded toward the broken cudgel. “The knee is a complex hinge. So is the will.”

A low groan came from Ulrich. It was a sound of pure animal pain, trapped behind clenched teeth.

“He lives,” the man said, answering the question I had not asked. “For now. His life is a currency. We are here to discuss its price.”

He moved then, not toward me, but along the side of the press. His fingers trailed over the cold iron of the screw, the smooth oak of the platen. It was a lover’s caress. He knew this machine. He understood its pressure, its capacity to flatten.

“You printed a calendar,” he said. “A harmless thing. Saints’ days, phases of the moon. But you used a particular stock. You asked a particular paper-maker about its origin. You followed a thread.” He stopped, his hand resting on the feed board. “You should know, Thomas, that some threads are not meant to be followed. They are meant to be cut.”

He lifted the shears again. This time, he didn’t snick the air. He closed them slowly, the blades meeting with a soft, final kiss of steel.

“The paper,” I said. The glacier in my stomach was melting into a cold, swift river. “The indulgence. The one I burned.”

A ghost of a smile touched his lips. It was not a warm thing. “The substrate,” he corrected. “The vessel. You focused on the ink, the words. A novice’s error. The truth is in the pulp. In the linen rags that were beaten into nothingness. In the water that carried the memory. You went looking for the source of the water.”

He had been to the Moselle. He had stood by the millrace. He had taken a twist of straw from a thatched roof, or from a field, and tied a knot only a valley man would know.

“Who are you?” The question was ash in my mouth.

“I am a librarian,” he said. “Of a sort. My order collects certain stories. And we ensure they are never bound into the common volume. Your calendar… it was a footnote to a story we have been shelving for a very long time.”

Order. The word had weight. It was not a gang, not a guild. It was an order. With rules, and a purpose that stretched beyond theft or murder.

“What do you want?”

“The thread ends here,” he said. “You will stop your asking. You will forget the paper, the water, the mill. You will return to being a devil, and ink your fingers with harmless texts. You will live a long, quiet, unremarkable life.” He paused, his milky-calm eyes holding mine. “And in return, your friend here will keep his knees. And your family in the Moselle will remain a memory of peace, and not a lesson.”

The threat was not shouted. It was laid out like a theorem. Clear, logical, inevitable.

Ulrich made another sound. This one was a word, mangled but distinct. “Don’t.”

The librarian’s head tilted. “He has more sense than you. He felt the persuasion. He understands the geometry of consequence.”

I looked at Ulrich’s broad back, the tremor in his shoulders. I saw the straw knot. I saw my mother’s face, my father’s hands, the slow green river of home. The price of my knowing was written in their blood. This man was not a thief. He was a surgeon. He was here to amputate my curiosity before it infected the body of my world.

The hook was in me. Deeper than fear. It was a terrible, clarifying understanding. I had been scratching at the plaster of the world, thinking I might find brick or timber beneath. I had found a void. And things lived in it. Things with shears.

“How do I know you’ll keep your word?” I asked. My knife hand had gone numb.

“You don’t,” he said simply. “Faith is always a gamble. But consider the alternative a certainty.” He finally moved away from the press, walking toward me with a quiet, measured tread. He did not brandish the shears. They were just there, at his side. He stopped an arm’s length away. I could smell him: lye soap, iron, and beneath it, something dry and old, like parchment in a sealed vault.

He reached out, not with the shears, but with his empty hand. He took my wrist, his grip firm but not cruel. He turned my hand over, exposing the palm, stained with cheap ink and road dirt. With a precision that was horrifying, he placed the twist of straw in my centre.

“A reminder,” he said. “Of the source. And of the shears.”

He released me. He stepped around me, as if I were a piece of furniture. At the door, he paused. He looked back at Ulrich, still kneeling in the centre of the room.

“The persuasion will fade. In an hour, he will be able to stand. The pain is the memory. Ensure he does not forget.”

And then he was gone. The door did not close. He left it ajar, just as I had found it, framing the grey, weeping light of the alley.

The silence he left behind was a new kind of noise. It was the sound of a world grown smaller, harder, and infinitely more careful.

I rushed to Ulrich. I touched his shoulder. He flinched.

“Don’t… move me,” he gritted out. His face was the colour of old ash, beaded with sweat that had nothing to do with the room’s chill. “Knee. Dislocated. He… popped it back in. But the ligaments…”

“I’ll get a physician.”

“No.” The word was a command, forged in pain. “No outsiders. He’ll be watching. Just… help me to the pallet.”

It took an age. Every shift, every touch, drew a swallowed scream from deep in his chest. He was a heavy man, and agony made him heavier. When he was finally lying on his straw mattress, his leg propped, he looked ten years older. The tree-root face was splintered.

“The shears,” he breathed, staring at the ceiling.

“You saw?”

“I saw. Felt one blade at my throat. Cold. While the other… did the work on my knee.” He closed his good eye. The milky one stared on, unblinking. “He’s not a bravo. Not a hired knife. He’s a… a cleric of hurt. A theologian of pain.”

“He called himself a librarian.”

Ulrich’s laugh was a short, bitter puff of air. “Of course he did. He’s a keeper of secrets. And we’re just loose pages.”

I looked down at my hand. The straw knot lay in my palm. It was a tiny, fragile thing. It carried the scent of sun and grain and home. It was the most terrifying object I had ever held.

“He’s been to the Moselle.”

“I know,” Ulrich said. “He told me. While he was working on my knee. He described the mill. The colour of your mother’s wash as she hung it by the stream. He was very… precise.”

The cold river in my gut became an ocean. He had not just gone to the valley. He had stood and watched. He had taken notes.

“What do we do?” The question was a child’s.

Ulrich was silent for a long time. The rain tapped on the shutters. “What he says. We stop. We forget.”

“Can you?”

He turned his head, his good eye fixing on me. It was full of a profound, weary knowing. “Thomas, I am a beggar, a liar, and a sometime thief. I have survived by knowing when a wall is too high to climb. This is not a wall. This is the edge of the world. You look over it, you don’t come back. And they don’t just push you. They push everyone you ever loved.” He gestured weakly at the straw in my hand. “That’s not a gift. It’s a collar.”

The truth of it settled into the stones of the floor, into the timbers of the walls. The story was over. The curiosity that had been a fire in me since I first saw the strange, watermarked paper was now a crime. To think of it was to endanger. To speak of it was to condemn.

I spent the next hours in a numb parody of routine. I built up the fire. I heated water and made Ulrich a poultice of comfrey and witch hazel, the way my mother had taught me for sprains. I picked up the scattered calendar sheets from the mud outside, their saints’ days blurred into illegibility. I cleaned the press, wiping away non-existent dust with a fervour that was its own kind of prayer.

I was trying to ink over the last week of my life. To print a new version of myself: the obedient devil, the boy who asked no questions.

Ulrich slept, fitfully, his dreams making him twitch and mutter.

As evening bled into night, the pain easing its grip enough for coherence, he spoke again, his voice rough.

“The chain,” he said. “He unlocked it.”

I had forgotten. I went to the door. The heavy iron chain lay coiled on the floorboards. I picked it up. The padlock was indeed fastened, the hasp closed. It had not been forced.

“He had a key,” I said.

“Or he was the key,” Ulrich murmured. “A man like that… locks are a formality. A courtesy he extends to the world.”

I turned the lock over in my hands. It was a solid thing, a fist of iron. Its mechanism was a mystery to me. But to the librarian, it was an open book. I thought of the shears. Of the knee. Of the straw. He understood the hidden mechanics of things—of joints, of locks, of loyalties. He applied pressure to the precise point that would cause the whole system to fail.

I was such a system. And he had found my point.

That night, I did not sleep. I sat by the banked fire, the straw knot in my hand. I traced the familiar pattern with my thumb. I thought of the story I had been trying to read. A story about paper. About water from a specific place giving life to a specific lie. A lie so important that an order of librarians would dislocate knees and threaten mothers to keep it buried.

To stop asking was a spiritual surrender. It was to look at the universe and agree to see only the plaster, to deny the void behind it. It was to become one of the living dead, a soul that had felt the hook and chosen to swallow it.

But to keep asking was a death sentence—for Ulrich, for my family, for myself. It was the sin of pride, the belief that my need to know was worth their blood.

I was caught between two kinds of damnation.

Just before dawn, as the room turned from black to grey, Ulrich spoke from his pallet. He was awake, staring at the ceiling.

“When I was a young man,” he said, his voice a dry rustle in the dark, “before the road, before this,” he gestured vaguely at his milky eye, “I wanted to be a cartographer. I loved the idea of drawing the edges of things. Of making the unknown known.” He paused. “Then I met a man who had been to the edge of a map. A place the charts marked only with serpents and the words Here Be Monsters. He wasn’t eaten by serpents. He was just… empty. As if the monsters hadn’t attacked him, but had simply breathed on him, and the breath had put out his candle. He said the worst thing wasn’t the monster. It was the knowing that the map was a lie. That the edge wasn’t an edge at all. It was just a place where the drawing stopped, and the real world, the endless, unmappable world, began.”

He turned his head toward me. “You have to decide, Thomas. Can you live with the lie of the map? Or does the truth of the emptiness call you, even if it means your light goes out?”

I had no answer. The straw knot bit into my palm.

The next day was a ghost of our old life. I went out for bread and cheese. The city seemed different. The shouts of the hawkers, the clang of the smithies, the rumble of carts—it was all a performance now. A play staged on a thin crust over a great dark. I saw ordinary men with tools—carpenters with chisels, cobblers with awls—and my blood ran cold. Any one of them could be a librarian. The unremarkable face was the perfect disguise.

When I returned, Ulrich was on his feet, leaning heavily on a makeshift crutch, his face pale but set. He was staring at the cold press.

“We have a job,” he said. “A real one. Pastor Gessel wants fifty catechisms. Simple text. No art. Harmless.”

It was a lifeline. A return to normalcy. A chance to ink over the void.

We worked in near silence. I set the type, my hands moving by rote. The familiar, monotonous click-clack of the sorts in the composing stick was a lullaby. I lost myself in the rhythm of it. In the beginning was the Word. I arranged the letters. And the Word was with God. I justified the line. This was my world. This small, finite, knowable world of lead and oak and black ink.

For three days, we were the men we had pretended to be. Ulrich healed. I printed. We spoke of the weather, of the price of lamp oil, of the vulgarity of the new songs in the taverns. We did not speak of shears, or straw, or librarians.

On the fourth day, I went to deliver the finished catechisms to Pastor Gessel’s church. It was a clear, cold afternoon, the sky the colour of a washed slate. The church was quiet, smelling of wax and stone and damp wool. Gessel was in his vestry, a small, dry man with ink-stained fingers of his own. He counted the booklets, nodded, and paid me in small coin.

As I turned to leave, he said, “A man was asking after you.”

The words were ice down my spine. “A man?”

“Mm. Yesterday. Said he was a binder from Leipzig, looking for reliable printers for devotional works. Asked if I knew of a shop with a skilled devil.” Gessel shrugged. “I mentioned you. He seemed… knowledgeable.”

“What did he look like?” I tried to keep my voice casual.

Gessel waved a hand. “Oh, you know. Average. Forgettable. Pleasant enough. He did have an interesting question, though.”

I waited. The vestry seemed to grow smaller.

“He asked if the paper we used for the catechisms was local. I said it was, from the mill by the Elbe. He smiled and said, ‘Ah, good. The river here is… tranquil. Some waters carry restless spirits.’ A odd thing to say, I thought. But you know these travelling men. Full of whimsy.”

The world tilted. Restless spirits. He wasn’t asking. He was telling. He was telling Gessel, and Gessel was telling me. He was still here. He was still watching. The performance was not over. My obedience was being reviewed.

I walked back to the shop through streets that felt like a maze in a dream. Every passerby was a mask. Every shadow held a pair of shears.

When I arrived, Ulrich was at the table, sharpening our one good knife on a whetstone. The rhythmic shush-shush stopped when he saw my face.

“What?”

I told him. About the binder from Leipzig. About the restless waters.

Ulrich put the knife down. The sound was final. “He’s not done. He’s a librarian. He needs to shelve the volume. He needs to be sure the story is closed.”

“What does that mean?”

“It means,” Ulrich said, his voice heavy, “that a quiet life might not be enough. He might need a definitive ending. A… colophon.”

The word hung between us. In a book, the colophon is the statement at the end that tells who printed it, and where, and when. The end of the story.

The hook was not in the threat. It was in the uncertainty. The not-knowing if obedience was enough. The terrible understanding that the only way to truly close the book might be for the main character to stop existing.

That night, for the first time, I did not take out the straw knot. I left it hidden under a loose floorboard. Its comfort was a poison. Its memory was a shackle.

I lay in the dark, listening to Ulrich’s snores, to the settling of the timbers. I thought of the librarian’s calm, forgettable face. I thought of his perfect, terrifying knowledge. He knew my home. He knew my trade. He knew the mechanics of Ulrich’s knee and the mechanism of my fear.

And I knew nothing of him. Not his name, not his order, not the shape of the story he was so desperate to keep unread.

That was the hook, sharper than any shears. The story was still there. It existed. It was real. And it was so dangerous that angels of silence with tools of pain were deployed to guard it.

To live was to agree never to read it. To die might be to finally turn the page.

The choice was not between safety and danger. It was between two kinds of oblivion. One was a warm, quiet forgetting. The other was a cold, final silence.

As the watchman called the hour, I realized the librarian had given me the straw knot not as a reminder of home, but as a sample of my own soul. Something small, dry, and easily cut.

And I knew, with a certainty that was worse than fear, that I could not live with the map. The lie of it would be a slow suffocation. The emptiness beyond its edge called to me with the voice of every restless spirit in every forbidden stream.

The hook was set. The line was taut.

The next chapter would be written not in ink, but in the choice between the shears and the abyss.

Chapter 17

CHAPTER SEVENTEEN

The silence after the storm was a different kind of noise. It was the sound of a held breath, of a city listening to its own damp heartbeat. The mud in the alley behind the scriptorium had not yet crusted; it was a black, clinging gum that whispered shuck-shuck with every step. My own breath plumed in the predawn murk, a ghost leaving my mouth on a regular schedule, as if to prove the machine still worked.

Ulrich was a heavier shadow beside me, his movements stiff, deliberate. The librarian’s knowledge of his bad knee was a phantom pain between us, a third presence in the dark. We did not speak. The hook the man had set was not in our throats but in our minds, a barbed question that tore if you turned your head too fast: What is a life not worth reading?

We were not there to steal. Not exactly.

The scriptorium was a low, timber-framed building hunched between a tannery—its stink a low, animal mourning in the air—and a granary. It was a place of copying, not creation. A factory for safe thoughts. Its shutters were bolted. The door was oak, iron-bound. It was not meant to keep us out. We were vermin; it was meant to make our entry noisy, a confession in splinters.

“The gutter,” Ulrich grunted, his good eye scanning the roofline.

A lead rain-channel ran from the steep pitch of the granary, dipped, and vanished into the scriptorium’s wall. A scribe’s concession to practicality over sanctity. It was wide enough for a rat. Or a desperate man.

The climb was a liturgy of small, terrible sounds. The grit of my fingers on wet timber. The groan of a rusted bracket. The soft, wet pop of the scab on my palm reopening. I was a blot moving against a darker page. Ulrich followed, his breath a pained sawing behind me, each heave of his body a defiance of the librarian’s clinical prediction.

The gutter mouth was a black O in the stone. It smelled of decayed leaves and stagnant water. I wriggled in, shoulders scraping, the world narrowing to a tunnel of cold, slimy metal. Then I was through, dropping onto a pile of discarded parchment scraps in a corner of an attic storeroom.

The room was a cathedral of forgotten words. Piles of damaged codices, their spines broken, slumped against each other like drunkards. Buckets of spoiled ink gave off a metallic reek. Sheets of imperfect vellum, scraped too thin or marred by hair-follicles, lay in stiff, ghostly stacks. This was the charnel house of the scriptorium, where imperfect truths were sent to molder.

Ulrich landed beside me with a thud that shook dust from the rafters. He did not speak, but his milky eye seemed to catch the faint grey light seeping through a crack, holding it like a coin.

We descended a narrow stair, into the belly of the beast.

The main hall was a long, cold nave. Rows of sloping desks faced north, for the steady light. Each stood empty, a tombstone awaiting its epitaph. Inkwells, clean and dry. Pumice stones for smoothing vellum. Knives for trimming. Quills, standing in pots like stripped feathers. It was a room of potential, frozen. The air tasted of chalk, of pumice, of the faint, sweet-sour tang of the size used to prepare parchment. It was the smell of preparation, of endless, patient getting-ready.

It was wrong.

A scriptorium sleeps, but it does not dream empty. There should be the whisper of a late-working illuminator in a side cell, the snore of an apprentice monk guarding the drying sheets. There should be the low, residual warmth of human industry, like embers in ash.

This was a cold hearth.

“He knew,” I whispered. The words were feathers, but they fell like stones.

Ulrich’s hand closed on my arm. His fingers were iron. He nodded toward the far end of the hall, where a heavier door stood ajar. The Master’s cell. The corrector’s room.

We moved between the desks, our shadows stretching long and thin ahead of us. The silence was a lens, focusing every shift of leather, every catch of breath. The open door was a mouth. We stood at its threshold, listening to its exhalation: more silence, and the faint, dry scent of cedar oil.

Inside, a single candle guttered on a stand, its light painting frantic shadows up the walls. The room was a perfect cube of order. A broad desk. A stand for an open codex. A rack of quills, graded by size. A mortar and pestle for grinding pigments. A square of clean white sand for blotting.

On the desk lay a single sheet of vellum. It was not large. It was creamy, flawless, stretched taut by its own drying memory of the calf.

It was not blank.

A single line of text was inscribed in the center, in a hand so precise it seemed etched, not written. The ink was a black so deep it drank the candlelight.

Ἐν ἀρχῇ ἦν ὁ λόγος.

In the beginning was the Word.

Below it, centered with a geometric cruelty, was a small, dry, perfectly-rendered drawing in iron-gall ink. A straw knot.

My blood became river-ice. The map in my boot sole seemed to burn through leather and wool. This was not a message. It was a colophon. A statement of authorship. I was here. I made this. I own your beginning, and I will write your end.

Ulrich made a sound in his throat, the growl of a wolf that sees the trap before it springs. He stepped back, his bad leg buckling slightly, and his hip knocked against the sand tray. It tipped. White sand hissed across the floorboards, a sudden, soundless avalanche.

In the perfect silence, the hiss was a scream.

From the deep shadow in the corner of the room, where a reading lectern stood, a shape unfolded. It did not rise; it uncoiled, as if from a state of perpetual readiness.

The librarian.

He wore a simple dark robe, no different from before. His face was still calm, forgettable. But here, in his own curated silence, he seemed larger. The candlelight found the planes of his face and made them continents of shadow. He held no shears. He needed none.

“Punctuality,” he said. His voice was the same dry, papery tone. It filled the room without seeming to rise above a murmur. “A virtue in a scribe. A curiosity in a thief.”

“We are not thieves,” I said. My voice was the young, thin one from the pilgrim road. It shamed me.

“You are in a place you do not belong, in the hour before God’s own light, with intent to take what is not yours.” He tilted his head, a scholar considering a flawed translation. “The semantics are trivial. The sin is in the action.” His eyes moved to Ulrich, then back to me. “You came for the story. The one that must not be read.”

“You left the map,” I said. The accusation was a weak defense.

“I left a test. A line of scripture. To see if you would trace it.” He gestured to the vellum on the desk. “You have. You stand at the beginning. The only question that remains is the nature of the end.”

Ulrich shifted his weight, a fighter’s adjustment. “We want no trouble. We walk away. The story stays shut.”

“Do you?” The librarian’s gaze was a pin, holding me to the specimen board. “Brother Thomas of the restless spirit and the forbidden streams? Do you walk away from the abyss once you have heard its voice? You told me you could not. Your face tells me you have not.”

He took a single step forward. The sand whispered under his foot. “The hook is set. You feel it. The line is taut. You are here. The only remaining act is the landing. Or the breaking.”

“What do you want?” The words were torn from me.

“I want the silence to remain unbroken. I am its custodian. You are a vibration in that silence. A dissonance.” He looked at the straw knot on the vellum. “You carry a little piece of noise with you, from your home, from your past. It is a seed. It could grow into a shout. I cannot allow that.”

“So you kill us?” Ulrich’s hand drifted toward the knife at his belt.

“Kill you?” The librarian almost smiled. It was a thin, academic stretching of lips. “That is one kind of ending. A period. But it is… inelegant. A dead man can become a martyr. A story. His absence can be louder than his presence.” He clasped his hands before him. “No. I prefer the colophon. The statement of completion. The rendering of a life into a closed book that no one will ever open.”

He looked at me. “You have two choices, as I see it. You can take the shears. The quiet, warm forgetting. You leave Wittenberg. You go to some other city, you drink, you tell small stories for coin, you grow old and your memories blur and the map becomes a quaint scar and the knot a forgotten trinket. You live a long, quiet, unread life. That is one colophon.”

The candle flame dipped, recovered. The shadows lunged and retreated.

“Or,” he continued, “you can pursue the story. You can try to find it. To read what must not be read. And in doing so, you will write your own ending. It will be swift. It will be final. And it will be written not by you, but by the silence you sought to break. A colophon in blood and oblivion.”

He was offering me a choice between two sentences. One long, rambling, and forgotten. One short, brutal, and definitive. Both written by him.

“Why?” I asked. The question was a child’s. “Why is it so dangerous? What is in it?”

For the first time, a flicker of something crossed his placid face. Not anger. Not fear. It was the profound distaste of a master calligrapher watching a novice blot a sacred text. “The story is not in it. The story is it. Some truths are not containers for meaning. They are meaning itself, given form. To read it is not to comprehend it. It is to be infected by it. To have the architecture of your soul rearranged to its grammar. It is a spiritual pestilence. My order was founded to contain it. To bind it in silence. To ensure the world remains… legible in its current, safe translation.”

He was not guarding a secret. He was guarding a contagion.

“You’ve read it,” I said.

He did not answer. He did not need to. His life was the answer. His perfect, terrible calm. His forgettable face. He was not its keeper. He was its first victim. A man who had read the text and had become its living colophon, a human end-stop to its meaning.

Ulrich had heard enough. His patience, a thick rope worn thin over days of fear, snapped. “Tom. We go. Now.”

He turned, his body blocking me from the librarian’s gaze for a second. It was a shield of flesh and loyalty.

The librarian moved.

It was not the lunge of a soldier. It was the efficient, unhurried motion of a man reaching for the correct tool. From the sleeve of his robe, his hand emerged. Not with shears. With a stylus. A scribe’s tool, used for ruling lines or incising notes in wax. Its tip was not sharpened to a point, but to a flat, cruel chisel-edge.

He did not strike at Ulrich’s heart or throat. As Ulrich turned his bad leg to push me toward the door, the librarian stepped in and drew the stylus, once, across the back of Ulrich’s working knee.

A short, precise line. Like a punctuation mark.

Ulrich made no sound. His leg simply ceased. It folded under him as if the tendon had been a piece of string, neatly severed. He crashed to the floor, his head striking the edge of the sand-scattered desk with a thick, wet crack. He lay still, a barrel felled, his good eye open and staring at the ceiling, seeing nothing.

The librarian stood over him, stylus poised, watching. A corrector reviewing a struck-through error.

A white-hot void opened in my mind. All thought, all fear, was burned away in its furnace. There was no hook. No line. No story. There was only Ulrich on the floor, and the man who had put him there.

I did not charge. I did not scream. I became a single, silent imperative.

My hand found the inkwell on the desk. Not the dry one. A smaller, lead-capped one for the corrector’s own use. I flung it. It was not an attack, but a distraction. The librarian’s head turned a fraction, his arm coming up to deflect the pot.

In that fraction, I was not where I had been.

I was at the pigment rack. My fingers closed not around a quill, but around the heavy marble mortar. I swung it in a short, tight arc. It was not a fighter’s swing. It was the desperate, brutal motion of driving a post into hard earth.

It connected with the side of his head.

The sound was wrong. Not a crack, but a damp thud, like a book dropped in mud.

He staggered. His perfect calm shattered into a mosaic of surprise and pain. He dropped to one knee, the stylus falling from his fingers, skittering across the sand-stained boards.

I stood over him, mortar raised, breath sawing in my throat. I was a boy from a drowned village, holding a stone. He was the angel of silence, kneeling in his own shattered sanctum.

He looked up. Blood, shockingly red, welled from his temple and traced a path through the dust on his cheek. His eyes were clear. The distaste was gone. In its place was a terrible, focused curiosity.

“Ah,” he breathed, the word a sigh of revelation. “The… alternative… colophon.”

He did not try to rise. He looked past me, to Ulrich’s still form. Then back to me. A smile, genuine this time, touched his bloody lips. It was the most horrifying thing I had ever seen.

“You have just… written your first… sentence.”

Then his eyes rolled back. He slumped sideways, collapsing into the spilled white sand, which began to drink the blood from his temple.

The silence rushed back in, a tidal wave. It was broken only by the ragged gasp of my own breathing and the soft, wet whisper of the candle.

I dropped the mortar. It hit the floor with a final, dull sound.

I crawled to Ulrich. His chest rose and fell, a shallow bellows. A gash bloomed on his forehead, seeping into his hairline. But he breathed. He lived.

The librarian lay still. I did not check if he breathed. I did not want to know.

I had come for a story and found a man. I had sought an ending and authored a beginning. A violent, stupid, irreversible beginning. The librarian was right. I had written a sentence. And then the keeper of silence was struck down with a stone.

It was not in any holy book. It was in mine. And it was the first line of a new chapter, written in blood and sand.

I hauled Ulrich up, draping his heavy arm over my shoulders. He was a mountain of dead weight. We stumbled from the corrector’s cell, through the nave of empty desks, back to the attic storeroom. The climb up the stairs was a torment. The journey back through the leaden gutter was a descent into a different hell.

We emerged into the grey, weeping dawn. The city was beginning to stir. A cart rumbled in a distant street. A dog barked.

We were two rats, fleeing a sinking ship of silence, one bleeding, one carrying the weight of a sentence that could never be unwritten.

I did not look back. I knew what was behind us. A man in sand. A sheet of vellum with a beginning and a symbol. A story that was a pestilence. And my own name, now irrevocably inscribed in its margin.

The hook was gone. I had swallowed it. The line was inside me. And I was the one now pulling, dragging the terrible, silent story behind me into the waking world.

Ulrich groaned, a deep, animal sound of pain. His eye flickered. “Tom?”

“I’m here.”

“Leg’s… gone.”

“I know.”

He was heavy. The road ahead was endless mud. The safe forgetting was lost. The warm oblivion was a lie left bleeding on a floor.

There was only the cold silence now, and the story within it. And I had just turned the page.

Chapter 18

CHAPTER EIGHTEEN

The mud of the pilgrim road was a different beast by day. It was not the slick, black river of our night flight, but a churned, grasping morass, studded with the sharp memories of cart-ruts and hoof-prints. It clung. It sucked. Each step was a negotiation, a plea to the earth to release my boot, a heave against the weight of the man on my shoulder.

Ulrich’s breath was a wet rasp in my ear, a bellows with a torn leather. His good eye was slitted open, fixed on the grey smear of sky. The other, the milky one, was crusted shut with blood and dirt. He was not walking. He was a sack of meat and bone I was dragging, one blasphemous step at a time, away from the city.

“Tom,” he grunted, on the fourth or the fortieth step. A statement, not a question.

“I’m here.”

“The book.”

“Gone.”

“The man.”

I did not answer. The memory was a shard of ice in my gut: the soft, final sound of the mortar on the floor. The librarian’s stillness. The sand, pristine and accusing around him. I had authored a silence far deeper than the one he guarded.

“He’s gone, too,” I said. The words were ash.

Ulrich’s head lolled against mine. “Then we’re free of it.”

He believed it. In the simple economy of the road, a threat removed was a threat resolved. You outrun the wolf, you bind the wound, you move on. The story was a wolf. I had brained it with a stone. We were moving on.

I wanted to believe his arithmetic. My soul, a famished and credulous thing, clutched at it. Yes. It is over. You stopped it. You saved him. You are free.

But my body knew a different truth. It was the body that had held the mortar, that had felt the jarring impact travel up its arm. It was the body that now carried this new, profound weight—not just Ulrich’s bulk, but the leaden, sickening knowledge of a line crossed. A spiritual sum had been tallied, and I was forever in the red. The hook was not gone. It was buried in the meat of me, and the line ran back through the miles of mud, through the city gate, across the quiet scriptorium, and anchored itself in the chest of a man who spoke of pestilence.

We did not get far.

A league, perhaps, from the city’s reeking shadow, Ulrich’s leg finally betrayed him utterly. It was not the old wound, the one that gave him his rolling sea-gait. It was the new damage from the fall in the gutter, or the frantic climb, or the weight I had put on him. A tendon, a bone—something gave with a soundless snap inside him. He cried out, a short, sharp bark of surprise more than pain, and we went down together into the cold embrace of the road.

He lay on his back in the mud, breathing in great, shuddering gulps. His face was the colour of old parchment. I knelt beside him, the world swimming in a grey haze of exhaustion. The rain had softened to a miserable drizzle. It beaded on his eyelashes.

“It’s done, Ulrich. We stop.”

He shook his head, a faint tremor. “Not safe. They’ll… find him.”

“They won’t look for pilgrims. They’ll think it a thief. Or a falling-out among scholars.” I was building a story for him, a flimsy shelter of lies. I needed to believe its architecture myself.

“The book, Tom.” His good eye found mine, desperate with a clarity that pain had burned into him. “You didn’t burn it. You left it.”

I had. In my panic, hauling him upright, I had left the vellum sheet on the desk. The librarian’s final word, his “Beginning,” and the symbol beneath it. The seed of the pestilence, lying there in a pool of lamplight for any early-rising corrector to find.

A fresh wave of cold washed through me. “It’s just a page. They’ll think it his work. They’ll shelve it. Lose it.”

“It’s a story,” he whispered, as if I were a fool. “Stories don’t get lost. They wait.”

There was no arguing with the theology of a storyteller. I had no strength for it. I got my arms under him again, a groan tearing from my own throat, and hauled him from the main track. We half-slid, half-fell down a shallow bank into a copse of skeletal alders. The ground was softer here, carpeted in rotten leaves. It was a tomb, but it was hidden.

I made him as comfortable as I could, propping him against a mossy stump. I peeled back the sodden wool of his hose. His leg from the knee down was an ugly, swollen parody, the skin tight and shiny. I touched it, and he hissed through his teeth.

“Broken,” I said.

“Aye.”

“I need to set it. Find a splint.”

“Later.” He caught my wrist. His hand was hot. “Listen. The city. What do you hear?”

I listened. The drizzle on dead leaves. The distant, mournful low of a cow. The wind in the bare branches, a sound like a slow exhalation. No bells. No shouts. No hue and cry.

“Nothing,” I said.

“Exactly.” He let go of my wrist, his arm falling heavily. “The silence is still there. You didn’t break it. You just… moved it.”

He was right. The terrible, listening quiet of the library had not ended with the librarian. It had seeped out with us. It hung in this damp grove. It was in the way the birds did not sing. It was in the hollow space where the city’s usual morning clamour should have been. We had not escaped the story. We had become its setting.

I built a fire. It was a pathetic thing, a smoulder of damp twigs and leaf-mould that gave more smoke than heat. But it was a ritual. A claim of territory against the silence. I held my numb hands over it, watching the thin grey plume rise to be shredded by the branches.

Ulrich slept, or passed out. His breathing grew more regular, but his face was flushed. Fever was building its own fire inside him.

I sat with my back to a tree, the librarian’s words scrolling behind my eyes. A story that is a pestilence. It does not convince. It consumes. It replaces the world with its own logic. I had thought he meant the story on the page, the one with the blank spaces. But what if the pestilence was not the tale itself, but the act of seeking it? The hunger that drove a man to break a silence? My seeking had led me to violence. My spiritual hunger had ended in a meal of ashes and blood. Was that the consumption? Was I now the carrier?

The day wore on, grey and seamless. I must have slept, for I dreamed not of the library, but of the road to Wittenberg. The rain was falling, and Ulrich was asking me if I felt the story. But in the dream, I looked down and saw my feet were not feet, but blocks of ink-stained type, sinking into the mud with every step, leaving behind perfect, legible prints of words I could not read.

I woke with a jerk. The fire was dead. The light was failing. And Ulrich was staring at me, his good eye wide and bright with fever.

“It’s not a book,” he said. His voice was clear, too clear.

“What isn’t?”

“The story. The pestilence.” He tried to push himself up, winced, and fell back. “He was the keeper. Not of a book. Of a door.”

A chill that had nothing to do with the damp crept over my skin. “What door, Ulrich?”

“The one the story comes through.” He was looking past me, into the gathering gloom of the copse. “You don’t write a pestilence. You… invite it. You make a space for it. A silence. A blank page. A quiet man in a quiet room.” His head turned toward me. The fever burned in him like a candle behind glass. “You struck the keeper. Not the tale. You didn’t stop the story, Tom. You opened the door wider.”

The logic of it, warped and terrible, clicked into place. The librarian’s peace, his perfect stillness, was not just a preference. It was a discipline. A lock. His existence was a seal on the door of whatever that vellum page truly was. My violence had shattered the lock. My panic had left the page unattended. My flight had spread the silence, the fertile ground for the story to grow.

I had not contained it. I had propagated it.

“We have to go back,” I said, the words leaving me before I knew I had thought them.

Ulrich let out a weak, wet laugh. “Back? Look at me, boy. I’m a broken anchor. You’d drag me back through that mud to what? Confess? They’d hang you from the wall. Or burn you. A murderer of a holy brother.”

“I have to close the door.”

“With what? Another stone?” He coughed, a ragged, deep sound. “You’re not a keeper of silence. You’re a crack in the wall. You’re how the draft gets in.”

The truth of it was a physical blow. I was not the physician. I was the symptom. My seeking was the disease. I put my head in my hands. The smell of woodsmoke was in my clothes, in my hair. Beneath it, I could still smell the library: dust, wax, and the faint, sweet scent of sandalwood from the librarian’s cell.

“Then what?” My voice was a hollow thing. “We just wait for it?”

“We move,” he said, but the command had no force. “We get to the next town. Find a bonesetter. Lose ourselves in a different story.” He was retreating to the old road-magic, the belief that a change of scenery was a change of soul.

But the hook was in me. I could feel the line pull, not from Wittenberg, but from within. The story was no longer out there. It was in here. In the memory of the impact. In the weight of Ulrich on my shoulder. In the new, sickly silence of the world. I had sought a spiritual truth and found only the capacity for sin. The seeking itself was the flaw.

As full dark fell, I knew I could not stay. The copse was a trap. Ulrich’s fever was a beacon. If anyone came looking, they would find us here, a perfect, pathetic end to the tale: the murderer and his accomplice, rotting in a ditch.

I had to get him to shelter. To some semblance of safety. It was the only penance left to me.

“We’re going,” I said, standing. My body screamed in protest.

“Where?”

“There was a farmstead. A mile back, off the eastern track. We saw its light last night.”

“A mile,” he murmured. It might as well have been a hundred.

I rebuilt the fire, let him soak in its meagre warmth while I foraged for a splint. I found two relatively straight, stout branches. Using my belt and strips torn from the hem of my own cloak, I bound his leg. He swore, colourfully and with great specificity, his voice rising to a shout that echoed in the silent wood. When it was done, he was grey and trembling, bathed in a cold sweat.

The journey to the farmstead was a descent into a lower circle of hell. I carried him on my back, his arms around my neck, his broken leg sticking out. We were a monstrous, four-legged creature, stumbling through the black. Every root was a snare, every ditch a canyon. My world shrank to the next step, the next gasp of air, the next muffled curse from Ulrich.

When I saw the yellow, wavering glow of a candle in a window, I wept. The tears were hot and immediate, mixing with the rain on my face. It was not hope. It was the raw, animal relief of a beast sighting its den.

The farm was a poor place—a mean house of wattle and daub, a sagging barn, a midden heap that announced itself long before we saw it. I did not care. I staggered to the door and beat upon it with the flat of my hand.

A long silence. Then the sound of a bolt being drawn.

The door opened a crack, revealing a slice of a wary, weathered face and the glint of a knife held low. A man’s eyes, hard and tired, looked me up and down, then past me to the shape of Ulrich on my back.

“We’re pilgrims,” I croaked, the prepared lie the only words I had left. “My father… he fell. His leg. We need shelter. For the night. We can work. We have a little coin.”

The man said nothing. His eyes took in our mud, our desperation, the clear wrongness of us. He was calculating the risk, the cost. I saw the moment his Christian duty, or perhaps his greed for the promised coin, outweighed his suspicion. He stepped back, opening the door wider.

“Barn,” he grunted, nodding toward the darker shape across the yard. “Not the house. You can have straw. Water from the trough. No fire. I see a spark, you’re back in the rain.”

It was more mercy than I had expected. “Thank you. God bless you.”

He snorted, a sound without humour. “Work it off tomorrow. Both of you, if he’s able. Now get in. You’re letting in the damp.”

The barn was a cathedral of shadows, smelling of old hay, manure, and the sweet, dusty scent of grain. I laid Ulrich down in a stall thick with dry straw. He was shivering violently now, his teeth chattering. The fever had him in its grip.

The farmer’s wife, a silent, pinch-faced woman, brought a ragged blanket and a wooden bowl of thin, greasy broth. She did not meet my eyes. She set them down and left as if fleeing a contagion.

I got some of the broth into Ulrich. He swallowed reflexively. I wrapped the blanket around him, then lay down beside him, sharing what little heat my own battered body could muster. The rain drummed a steady tattoo on the roof. An animal shifted in the dark, stamping its hoof.

In the profound, exhausted stillness, Ulrich spoke again, his voice a thin thread in the dark.

“Tom.”

“I’m here.”

“When I was a boy… my village had a plague of stories.”

I went very still. He had never spoken of his boyhood.

“Not sickness,” he whispered. “Stories. A travelling player came. Told one tale. A simple thing. A love story. A betrayal. A ghost. Within a week, everyone was telling it. But… changed. The butcher saw the ghost in his smokehouse. The priest heard the betrayal in a confession. A girl drowned herself in the river, claiming she was the lover. The story… it ate the village. It became the only thing anyone could see. The player had left, but the story stayed. It was in the water. In the air.”

He coughed, a weak, tearing sound. “They burned the girl’s body. They rang the bells for a week. They told different stories, loud, from the pulpit. They tried to shout it down. But it was too late. The village was the story. I left. I’ve been leaving ever since.”

I understood now. His life on the road, his endless hunger for new tales, his fear of stillness—it was not wanderlust. It was exorcism. He was forever trying to outrun the story that had consumed his home, replacing it with ten thousand others before it could root. The librarian’s warning had struck a chord in him deeper than he’d let on.

“This is the same,” I said.

“No.” He turned his head toward me. In the faint light from a crack in the wall, his face was a skull etched in shadow. “Worse. That story… it was a ghost. It haunted us. This one… the one in the silence… I think it’s alive. And you fed it.”

I had no answer. The barn’s silence pressed in, thick and listening. It was the library’s silence. It was the silence of the copse. It was the silence inside me, where God’s voice had once been and now there was only the echo of a striking stone.

I slept. And in my sleep, I did not dream of words or mud or blood.

I dreamed of a door.

It was a simple door, of aged, polished oak, set in a stone wall that stretched to infinity in either direction. There was no handle. No keyhole. Before it, on the floor, was a small, neat pile of sand.

I knew, with the absolute certainty of dreams, that this was the door Ulrich spoke of. The door the keeper kept. And I knew I had to open it. I had to see what was on the other side. My spiritual seeking, my cursed, insatiable need to know, propelled me forward.

I reached for it.

Just as my fingers brushed the warm wood, I saw words begin to bleed across its surface, not carved, but forming from the grain itself. They were in no language I knew, yet I understood them. They were the title of the story. The name of the pestilence.

And they were my name.

I woke with a gasp, sitting bolt upright in the straw. Dawn was a grey smear through the cracks in the barn walls. Ulrich was asleep, his breathing a shallow, rapid flutter. His forehead was burning.

And outside, in the farmyard, there was a new sound.

Voices. Not the farmer’s gruff tones. Clearer, harder voices. Official voices. The sound of booted feet on wet earth. The jingle of harness.

I crawled to the barn door, pressed my eye to a gap in the planks.

Three men stood in the yard with the farmer. They wore the city’s livery. One held the reins of two mud-spattered horses. Their cloaks were dark with rain.

“…found at first light,” one of them was saying. His voice carried, crisp in the damp air. “A brother of the library. His head stove in. Something stolen from the scriptorium.”

The farmer was shaking his head, arms crossed over his chest. “Seen no one. Heard nothing. It’s a quiet road.”

The guardsman’s eyes scanned the yard, the house, the barn. They passed over the crack where I watched. “You’re certain? Two men, likely. One older, lame. The other younger. They would have come this way. They’d be desperate. Perhaps injured.”

My blood turned to ice. They knew. They knew it was two. They knew of Ulrich’s leg. They had found the librarian. And they had found the page missing. Something stolen. The vellum. They thought it a theft. They did not understand it was a release.

“I keep to myself,” the farmer said, his voice final. “If I see such men, I’ll send word.”

The guardsman nodded, not believing him. He gave the barn one last, long look. “See that you do. The Archbishop himself is interested. Calls it a sacrilege. A stain on the word of God.” He swung himself up onto his horse. “There’s a reward for their capture. A substantial one. Enough to buy a great deal of quiet.”

He let the words hang in the air. Then he turned his horse, and the three of them splashed back out onto the track, heading east, the way we had come.

I slid down the barn door, my heart hammering against my ribs. The farmer would not send word. He would come himself. For the reward. He would wait until the guards were out of sight, then he would come for us. We were coin to him now. A means to a quieter life.

The false victory of our escape lay in ruins around me. We were not safe. We were a commodity. The story was not just a spiritual pestilence. It was a worldly one, too. It had a price on its head. And my name was on the door.

Ulrich was awake, watching me. He had heard.

“The hook,” he said softly. “It’s not just in you, boy. It’s in the world now. And it’s reeling us in.”

I looked at him—broken, burning, utterly dependent. I looked at the barn door, beyond which a farmer was now weighing a knife against a promise of gold. I looked at my own hands, the hands that had held the mortar.

The seeking was over. I had found what I sought. It was a door with my name on it. And the only choice left was whether to wait for it to open, or to walk through it of my own will.

“We have to move,” I said. But the words were different now. They were not flight. They were a direction.

“Where to?” he asked, the old question. But his eyes held a new, grim understanding.

I did not answer the city. Or the next town. Or any place on any map.

I answered the only truth left.

“Into the story,” I said.

Chapter 19

CHAPTER NINETEEN

The farmer did not come that night.

We waited in the dark of the barn, the air thick with the smell of old hay and our own sour fear. The rain had stopped, leaving a silence that was worse than the storm. Every creak of the settling timber, every scuttle of a rat in the straw, was the slide of a bolt, the crunch of a boot. I held the heavy iron mortar like a child clutches a doll, its weight the only solid thing in a world turned to liquid treachery. Ulrich lay still, but his breathing was all wrong—shallow, quick, a bellows with a torn leather. The fever was baking him from the inside out.

Dawn came not with light but with a slow, grey leaching of the dark. The high window showed a slab of colorless sky. The farmer’s house remained shut, smoke rising thin and straight from its chimney. A normal morning. A lying morning.

“He’s waiting for full light,” Ulrich whispered. His voice was a dry leaf skittering on stone. “Or for his sons to return from the market. Strength in numbers. Profit is a patient man.”

I had not slept. My thoughts were not thoughts but a single, grinding wheel. A door with my name on it. The Archbishop’s men wanted the vellum, the physical sacrilege. The farmer wanted the reward, the worldly weight of it. And I… I carried the story. It was the hook, as Ulrich said. It was in the world now, and it was reeling us in. To walk into it was not a metaphor. It was the only road left that was not a trap.

“Can you stand?” I asked.

He tried. The sound he made was a soft, wet tearing, deep in his chest. He collapsed back into the straw, his face the color of ash. “The legs are willing,” he gasped. “The body is not.”

I looked at the barn door, a rectangle of planks bound by iron. It was a barrier. It was a coffin lid. The story was not a place for a dying man. It was a current. It would drown him.

And yet, to leave him was to nail that coffin lid shut myself.

I moved. There was no more debate. Debate was for men with choices. I tore a long strip from the bottom of my own threadbare tunic and filled the iron mortar with water from our skin. I soaked the cloth and pressed it to Ulrich’s forehead, his neck. The heat coming off him was a physical pressure.

“Stop,” he croaked, trying to bat my hand away. “The boy playing nurse. Save the water.”

“I am not saving it,” I said, and the coldness in my own voice startled me. “I am spending it. To buy you time to stand.”

I went to the barn door, put my eye to a gap. The farmyard was empty. A single chicken pecked at the mud. The door of the house opened, and the farmer’s wife emerged, emptied a basin of slops, went back inside. No glance toward the barn. It was a performance of ignorance. It confirmed everything.

Back at Ulrich’s side, I forced more water between his cracked lips. I rewet the cloth. The minutes were grains of sand in an hourglass made of flesh. The story was not a patient thing. It was a hunger. And it was my hunger, too. I felt it, a hollowing ache beneath my ribs that had nothing to do with food. The need to know, to see the door, to walk through it. It was a worse fever than Ulrich’s.

The sun broke through, a pale, watery coin behind the clouds. It painted a weak bar of light across the barn floor. In that light, the dust motes danced like gnats. Like spirits.

Ulrich’s hand shot out and gripped my wrist. His strength was a phantom, a last surge from a dying fire. “Listen to me,” he hissed. His good eye burned into mine. “This tale you seek… it is not a sanctuary. A story that can rot vellum, that can make an Archbishop hunt a beggar… it is a crack in the world. You look into it, it looks back. And what looks back is not God.”

“What is it, then?”

“A question,” he said, his voice dropping to a whisper. “One that eats its own answers. I have carried you toward it because it was your wish. But I am old, and I am broken, and I see now. I am the anchor you must cut loose.”

“No.”

“Yes. Take your mortar and your madness and go. Out the back. Through the fields. The farmer comes for us. For the pair. You alone are just a vagrant. A nothing. You can slip through the net.”

His words were sense. They were the cold, clear logic of the road. A brother cuts loose a sinking weight. It is the first law.

But the story was not of the road. It was underneath it.

“You said the hook was in the world,” I said, prying his fingers from my arm. They were hot and dry. “It is not in me alone. It has caught you, too. You heard it in the rain on the Wittenberg road. You feel it now. It is reeling us in. Not me. Us.”

He stared at me, and for a moment the fever seemed to clear, burned away by a terrible, lucid fear. He saw I meant it. He saw I would not go.

“Then you are a fool,” he said, but there was no force in it. Only a weary surrender.

The bar of sunlight had climbed the far wall. It was time.

I hauled him upright. He was a sack of bones and fever, his weight shocking in its totality. I got his arm around my shoulders, my own arm locked around his waist. The pain in my feet, my legs, my soul, vanished under the sheer physical impossibility of it. We took a step. Then another. We were not walking. We were a single, lurching creature, a broken puppet with tangled strings.

I did not head for the main door. In the night, my eyes had traced the lines of the barn. There was a smaller door at the rear, for carting manure to the midden. It was bolted from the inside, rusted shut.

We fell against it. I fumbled with the bolt, my fingers slick with Ulrich’s sweat. It gave with a shriek that seemed to tear the morning in two.

The world outside was a shock of open air and sloping field. A path, thick with mud, led down to a copse of skeletal winter trees. Beyond that, the land rose again, vague in the morning haze.

“Go,” I grunted, and we went.

We were a snail’s track of agony across the open field. Every step was a prayer and a curse. I expected a shout at any moment. The bang of a door. The whistle of a crossbow bolt. But there was only the suck of the mud, Ulrich’s ragged breath, and the drumbeat of my own heart.

We reached the trees. I let him slide down against an oak, its bark like ancient, grey skin. I turned back to look.

The farm was a toy in the distance. The barn door was still shut. And then, as I watched, the front door of the house opened. The farmer stepped out. He did not look toward the barn. He looked straight out across the field, toward the copse. He had a wood-axe in his hand. He stood for a long moment, a still, dark figure against the whitewash of his home. Then he turned and walked, not toward the barn, but around the side of the house. Toward the path that led to the road. To fetch his sons. To gather his net.

We had minutes.

“The story, Ulrich,” I gasped, hauling him up again. “Where does it live? Not in a city. Not in a book. Where?”

He was fading. His head lolled against my shoulder. “The old tales… the ones they tried to scrub from the stone… they speak of a Nebelkloster,” he murmured. “A Cloister of the Mist. Not a place on a map. A place the map avoids. Where the river forgets its name and the rocks hold their breath.”

“Where?”

“Follow the hook,” he slurred. “It’s in your gut, boy. It’s the pull… the empty place that wants filling…”

It was nothing. A fireside ghost story. But it was the only compass I had.

We pushed deeper into the wood. The ground began to rise. The trees grew thicker, the light dimmer. The world of farms and roads and Archbishop’s men fell away, swallowed by rotten leaves and silent, watching trunks. We were in the throat of the land now. And the land did not care.

Ulrich’s steps grew heavier. His weight became absolute. We were climbing a low, rocky ridge when his legs finally gave way. We went down in a tangle of limbs and agony, sliding in the wet leaf-mold. He did not get up.

I rolled him over. His eyes were closed. His breathing was a shallow, fluttering thing, like a bird caught in a chimney.

“Ulrich.”

No answer.

I shook him. I slapped his face, lightly at first, then harder. “Brother. Look at me.”

His eyelid flickered. The milky eye stared at the canopy. The good one found me, but it was looking from a great distance. “The rain,” he whispered. “It’s stopping.”

There was no rain.

“Stay with me,” I begged. The words were ash in my mouth. “The Cloister. We’ll find it.”

A faint, bloody smile touched his lips. “You will. I… I think I found my quiet place.” He drew a breath that seemed to cost him everything. “The story, Thomas. It’s not in the mist. It’s in the seeking. The crack… mind you don’t fall…”

His hand twitched in the leaves. Then it was still.

The woods were very quiet. No bird sang. No wind stirred the bare branches. There was only the sound of his absence, a new, vast silence where his breath and his voice had been.

I knelt in the mud beside him. I closed his good eye with my thumb. The rain began then, a soft, cold drizzle that beaded on his lashes and ran down the gullies of his face like tears. God’s own punctuation.

I do not know how long I stayed. Time became the falling rain, measureless and cold. He was my brother of the road. He had carried me, taught me, lied for me. He had been my last tether to the world of men and miles and tangible things. Now the tether was cut. I was adrift in the story, and the story was a wide, grey sea.

The hook in my gut pulled. It was a physical pain, a cramp of longing that doubled me over. It was not grief. It was deeper than grief. It was the story, hungry, impatient. It had used Ulrich to bring me this far. Now it had no more need of him.

I stood. My body was a stranger’s body, stiff and cold. I took the iron mortar from where it had fallen. I looked down at the man who was no longer a man, just a shape in the leaves. I had no spade. No words. The earth was too hard, the roots too thick.

The story offered no rites. It only consumed.

I turned my back on him and began to climb.

The ridge gave way to a high, wild moor. The rain misted down, blending earth and sky into a single, damp grey wool. Visibility was a stone’s throw. Less. The world had been reduced to the next step, and the next. The hook pulled me north and east, away from the sun’s hidden path. I followed it. It was my only star.

I walked for hours. The moor was a trackless waste of heather and gorse and sudden, sucking bogs that gleamed like pewter. My ruined boots were swallowed twice; I left them buried in the greedy mud and walked on in my sodden foot-cloths. The cold was a sharp, clarifying pain. It burned away thought, leaving only instinct and that relentless, inward tug.

As the day faded into a premature twilight, the land began to change. The heather fell away. The ground underfoot became rocky, scattered with stones that seemed too smooth, too round, as if worn by a river that was no longer there. The mist thickened, coiling around my knees like a living thing. It had a smell—not damp earth, but something older, flinty and cold, like the air deep inside a cave.

And then I saw the first stone.

It was a pillar, rough-hewn, twice my height, leaning at a drunken angle. It was not a natural thing. Moss and lichen crusted it, writing a green, wordless script across its face. I touched it. The stone was ice-cold, and for a fleeting second, the murmur in my blood—the one that had begun with the vellum—rose to a faint, chiming resonance, then faded.

I moved past it. Another stone appeared from the mist, then another. They formed no clear circle, no path. They were markers. Or tombstones. Or the rotten teeth of something long buried.

The ground sloped down into a shallow valley. The mist pooled here, a white, silent lake. And rising from its center were shapes.

Walls.

Not complete walls. Broken stubs of masonry, no higher than my chest, tracing the ghost of a rectangle in the earth. A foundation. A memory in stone. Within the rectangle, the mist was thinner. I could see flagstones, cracked and heaved by frost and root. At the far end, one archway remained standing, a skeletal rib of stone against the grey sky. It led to nothing.

Nebelkloster.

This was not a cloister. It was a corpse. The silence here was not an absence of sound. It was a presence. It pressed against my ears, a weight. My own breathing was obscenely loud.

The hook in my gut was silent. The pull was gone. I had arrived.

I walked across the broken flagstones. My feet, numb with cold, felt every seam, every unevenness. In the center of the space, the flagstones were darker, stained with something time had not been able to bleach away. A hearth? An altar?

I stood in the middle of it. The mist curled around the broken walls. The single arch watched me like a blind eye.

Nothing happened.

There was no door. No revelation. No voice from the mist. There was only ruin, and cold, and a vast, crushing disappointment that tasted of iron on my tongue. Ulrich had died for a fireside tale. I had walked into the story, and the story was empty.

I sank to my knees on the cold stone. The iron mortar fell from my hand with a clang that was swallowed instantly by the waiting silence. I had sought a crack in the world. I had found a hole in the ground. The laugh that tore from my throat was a short, ugly sound. It died without an echo.

I was nothing. A vagrant. A fool. A murderer of old men. The story was not a door. It was a pit, and I was at the bottom of it.

I bowed my head. The drizzle kissed the back of my neck.

And then I saw it.

Between two cracked flagstones, right before my knees, something gleamed. Not wet. Not stone. A different black. An organized black.

I reached down, my fingers numb and clumsy. They closed not on a thing, but on an edge. I pulled. It resisted, then came free with a gritty sigh.

It was a piece of vellum.

Small, no larger than my palm. Torn, ragged, blackened at the edges as if by fire. But in its center, protected, was writing. The same hand. The same ink. The same language that was no language.

I held it. The murmur in my blood did not rise. It stopped. It ceased entirely, replaced by a perfect, ringing stillness. The world—the mist, the stones, the cold—seemed to hold its breath.

This was not a page from a book. It was a fragment. A shard.

I turned it over.

On the other side, scrawled in a different, frantic, ink-stained hand—a hand I knew—were words in German. Latin. Greek. A palimpsest of desperation.

…the name is not a name it is a key… …the mortar is not for grinding it is for hearing… …the mist is not a veil it is a page… …do not read it listen…

My own words. From my own journal. The one I had kept in the scriptorium. The one I had burned.

But I had not written this. Not here. Not on this.

The vellum in my hand was centuries older than me.

A sound broke the silence. Not from the fragment. From the world. A soft, wet crunch of a foot on stone.

I looked up.

A figure stood in the broken archway.

It was not the farmer. Not a guardsman. It was a man in a robe, hooded, the cloth the color of the mist and the stone. He stood perfectly still. I could not see his face.

He did not speak. He simply raised one arm, a slow, deliberate movement, and pointed. Not at me. At the iron mortar where it lay on the flagstones.

Then he turned and walked, soundlessly, into the mist beyond the archway and was gone.

I looked at the mortar. I looked at the fragment of vellum. I looked at the archway, empty now.

The hook was not in my gut anymore.

It was in my hand. And it was turning.

Chapter 20

CHAPTER TWENTY

The mist in the archway held the shape of him for a heartbeat longer, a ghost of a ghost, and then it was just wet stone and empty air. The silence he left behind was not the silence of absence. It was the silence of a drawn bowstring. The world did not resume. It waited.

My hand, the one clutching the vellum, had gone beyond cold into a kind of dead numbness. The other hand, resting on the wet flagstone, felt the ancient chill of the abbey seeping up, a slow poison. I looked from the empty archway to the mortar. A simple, ugly thing of pitted iron, squatting on the stone like a malevolent toad. He had pointed. Not at the secret it contained, but at the vessel itself. The cup of it was filled with rainwater and the black silt of centuries.

…the mortar is not for grinding it is for hearing…

My own words, but not my own. Written in my hand on a skin older than Luther’s heresy. The hook was turning. I could feel it, a slow, deliberate revolution in the center of my chest, winding something tight.

I pushed myself up. My knees protested, bones grinding like pebbles in a sack. The movement broke the spell. The murmur in my blood did not return, but the ringing stillness hardened into a focused quiet, the quiet of the scriptorium just before the first pen scratches at dawn. This was a task. A reading.

I knelt before the mortar. The rain had softened to a fine, persistent drizzle, beading on the iron. I did not touch it. I listened.

At first, there was only the drip of water from the broken cloister roof, the distant complaint of a crow. Then, beneath it, a sound so low it was less a sound and more a vibration in the teeth. A hum. It came from the mortar. Not from the hollow of it, but from the metal itself. A sustained, iron note, tuned to the weight of the rain, to the pressure of the air in the ruins. It was not for grinding. It was a sounding bowl. A resonator.

My eyes went to the fragment in my hand. The blackened vellum was beginning to soften in the wet, the edges curling. The unknown script in the center seemed to drink the grey light, the ink holding a dull sheen like a beetle’s back. On the reverse, my frantic polyglot scrawl was bleeding, the Latin audire blurring into the Greek ακούω.

Do not read it listen.

I had written that. I had burned that journal. I had watched the pages curl and blacken in the brazier’s heart, the secrets of my confusion turning to ash and smoke. This fragment should not exist. Yet its weight in my hand was the only true thing.

The hum from the mortar shifted pitch, a quarter-tone drop. My head came up. The mist beyond the archway had thickened, coiling around the broken columns like a lazy serpent. There was no figure. But the pointing finger was still there, in the air, in the intention of the thing. He had shown me the instrument. The fragment was the score.

I am a scholar of the word. I know the power of the spoken prayer, the chanted psalm, the whispered confession. Sound shapes spirit. It was a tool of God, and of the Enemy. This was older than both. This was the sound before the Word.

I laid the fragment on the ground before the mortar, the strange script facing up. I placed my hands on the cold, wet iron rim. The vibration traveled up my arms, settling in the hollows of my shoulders. I closed my eyes. I did not read the script. I let my gaze blur over it, absorbing the shapes not as letters but as notations, as marks of rhythm and stress. The hum of the mortar filled my skull. I began to voice the shapes.

It was not speaking. It was not singing. It was a toneless emission of breath, shaped by teeth and tongue into forms that felt alien in my mouth—clicks, glottal stops, a low drone from the back of the throat. The sounds meant nothing. They were architecture. They were keys.

As I made them, the hum from the mortar answered. It amplified them, deepened them, gave them a physical weight that pressed against the skin of my face. The drizzle seemed to bend around the space where I knelt, the drops falling in a perfect, silent circle just beyond the mortar’s rim. The light changed. Not brightened, but clarified, as if the mist was being combed into fine, parallel lines.

The script on the vellum began to move.

Not the ink. The space around the ink. The parchment itself seemed to recede, the black letters rising, not off the surface, but into a different layer of seeing. They floated in the air between the fragment and the mortar, rotating slowly. And as they turned, they unfolded. A line would split, become a branching tree of sub-glyphs. A curve would open like a flower, revealing intricate patterns within. It was a living grammar, a syntax of shapes breeding and dividing in the charged air.

I could not stop the sounds coming from my throat. They were pulling themselves out of me now, drawn by the resonating iron. I was the bellows. The mortar was the pipe.

The unfolded script began to align. Not into words I knew, but into a pattern. A map. It resolved into the outline of walls, of corridors, of a central space. The floorplan of a building. This abbey. But not as it stood in ruin. As it once was. Whole. And within the outline, three points glowed with a soft, phosphorescent light. One was here, in this cloister. The second was in the chancel, near the high altar. The third was deep below, in the undercroft.

The map hung in the air, shimmering. A guide. A trap. The hook turned another revolution, sharp and deep.

A gust of wind, cold and sudden, ripped through the cloister. It tore the map of light to tatters. It snatched the vellum fragment from the flagstones, sending it tumbling end over end into the shadows of the north wall. The connection broke. The hum from the mortar ceased as if severed with a knife.

The ordinary, brutal cold of the afternoon rushed back in. I slumped forward, my forehead striking the damp iron rim with a dull tonk. My throat was raw, scraped clean. The sounds were gone, but their echoes seemed to rattle in the empty vault of my skull.

I raised my head. The light was just the flat, grey light of a Saxon afternoon. The mist was just mist. The mortar was just a lump of iron.

But I had seen. I had spoken the unspeakable. And I had been shown.

I stumbled to my feet, my body aching as if I had fought a long, silent battle. I retrieved the fragment from where it had lodged against a pile of rubble. It was just a piece of old skin again, inert, the script once more flat and mysterious. But it was warm. As warm as living flesh.

I knew what I had to do. The map was in my mind now, etched there by the strange light. Three points. I was at the first. The next was the chancel.

I left the cloister, the mortar a forgotten sentinel in the rain. Moving through the ruins felt different. The stones were no longer just stones. They were pages in a book I had just learned the first syllable of. The very air felt scribbled upon, thick with latent meaning. I passed the farmer’s cart, still standing forlorn in the nave. The ox lowed softly, a mundane sound that seemed blasphemous now.

The entrance to the chancel was a great, jagged hole where the rood screen had been torn down. The roof here was mostly gone, open to the sky. Weeds grew between the flagstones where bishops had once trod. The high altar was a massive slab of broken marble, half-covered by a collapsed section of vaulting. The second point of light had glowed near here.

I approached slowly, the fragment a burning coal in my fist. The silence was profound. Even the rain seemed hushed. I stood before the wreckage of the altar, feeling nothing. No hum. No pull. This was wrong.

I closed my eyes, recalling the map. The light had not been on the altar. It had been to the left of it, near the wall. I picked my way over the fallen stone. The south wall of the chancel was scarred but still stood, patches of faded fresco showing the agonized faces of saints. I ran my hand over the cold, wet stone. Nothing.

Then I saw it. A single, small carving amidst the floral stonework of a pilaster. It was a mason’s mark, common enough—a simple, stylized letter M with a loop. But carved within the loop was a smaller, finer symbol: a spiral with three arms. The exact center of the spiral was a deep, drilled hole, no wider than a goose quill.

I brought the fragment close to the hole. Nothing happened. I put my eye to it. Darkness. I turned and looked back at the space before the altar, trying to triangulate the memory of the light. It had been here, in this very spot, but not in the wall. In the air.

The air.

…the mist is not a veil it is a page…

I looked up. The drizzle fell steadily through the open roof. I held out the fragment of vellum, letting the fine rain mist over its surface. The ink did not run. Instead, where the beads of water gathered on the strange central script, they caught the grey light and held it, tiny lenses. I tilted the fragment. The droplets rolled, merging, carving watery paths through the script.

And in the air above the fragment, reflected and magnified by the film of water, a ghost of the unfolded script returned. Faint. Trembling. It was not the map this time. It was a single, complex glyph, rotating slowly. It pulsed once, like a heartbeat, and then dissolved as the water sheeted off the vellum.
