We came to a chamber that was not a room, but a concept given space: Rectification.

It was vast, empty, and centred on a single, plain writing desk of dark wood. It was the only thing in this place that looked made by hands. Human hands. Behind the desk, in a high-backed chair, sat the Chronicler.

He was as I remembered from the riverbank, and yet utterly more. There, he had been a functionary in a hurry. Here, he was the function. His grey robe was the colour of un-inked vellum. His face was bland, forgettable, but his eyes held the flat, assessing light of a jeweller viewing a flawed stone. He did not look up as we entered. He was writing in a large, open folio with a quill that made no sound.

We stopped before the desk. The hum in my chest was now a deafening silence, a pulled thread that had reached its knot.

He finished his notation, set the quill in its well, and closed the folio with a soft thump that echoed in the metaphysical space. Then he looked at us.

“Thomas of Wittenberg,” he said. His voice was dry, precise, devoid of malice or interest. It was the voice of a clock announcing the hour. “Ulrich of the Roads. Captain Rensfeld. You are late.”

“Late for what?” My own voice sounded rough, animal, in this refined air.

“For your correction. The anomaly on the Sullen Walrus was logged. A standard mend was dispatched. It failed. The anomaly persisted. Then it moved. This required escalation.” He steepled his fingers. “You are a narrative instability. A propagating error. You have travelled beyond your designated parameters, armed with a… borrowed artifact.” His gaze flicked to my chest, where the wooden shape warmed against my skin. “You have achieved localised authorship. This is not permitted.”

Ulrich shifted his weight. “So you’ll wipe the slate? Un-write us?”

“Un-writing is inefficient. It leaves… gaps. Ripples. You will be corrected. Integrated. Your journey will be given a proper, harmonious conclusion. The mad scribe will find peace in a quiet cell. The old soldier will die nobly in a forgotten battle. The captain will go down with his ship in a storm that has, I see, already been written.” He gestured to a stack of glowing cubes nearby. “It is more elegant. The story retains its shape. Only the aberrant inflection is removed.”

Rensfeld’s hand went to his cutlass hilt. “You wrote my storm?”

“I am a scribe of the Third Order. I do not write the primary narrative. I ensure its continuity. Your storm was a necessary pressure valve in the maritime trade sub-plot of the Hanseatic League sequence. Your personal tragedy was a statistically insignificant component.”

The captain’s face went pale with a rage so pure it was almost holy. He took a step forward.

The Chronicler did not move. A pulse of silent energy emanated from the desk. Rensfeld froze, not in ice, but in a sudden, perfect stillness. He was not paralysed. He was paused. A single frame in a story.

“Do not,” the Chronicler said, his voice still calm, “introduce unnecessary drama. This is a place of revision, not theatre.”

He turned his eyes back to me. “You are the core. The scribe who learned to read the world’s text. You saw the pen strokes. A remarkable, if unfortunate, sensitivity. You will be the easiest to correct. The story of your life simply… ends. Here. A quiet cessation. The hook in your soul, as you poetically term it, will be snipped. You will feel nothing. You will be nothing. A closed folio.”

He reached for a new, blank sheet of vellum from a stack. He selected a different quill, this one tipped with a sliver of light so bright it hurt to see. He poised it over the page.

This was it. Not a battle. Not a grand argument. An editorial decision.

The warmth in my chest became a searing heat. The wooden walrus was not just glowing; it was singing, a high, clear counterpoint to the Archive’s deep hum. It was a story that did not belong here. A story of foolish hope, of a carved god, of a lie that saved.

The Chronicler’s brow furrowed, the first human expression I had seen on him. “The artifact. It should have been neutralised. Its narrative energy is… persistent.”

He began to write. The quill of light moved, and with each stroke, I felt a part of me begin to unlatch. The memory of the rain on the road to Wittenberg grew faint. The smell of Ulrich’s sweat and garlic seemed a dream. The feel of a ship’s deck underfoot—a fiction.

I was being un-made. Not with violence, but with a supreme, indifferent revision.

I fell to my knees. The plain floor was cold. I clutched at my chest, at the burning wood. It was not enough. It was a child’s story against the author of the world.

Then Ulrich spoke.

He did not address the Chronicler. He did not roar. He simply began to talk, his voice the familiar gravelly rumble, filling the silent, rectifying space.

“Let me tell you a story,” he said. “About a scribe, an old soldier, and a captain. It’s not a good story. It’s full of mud and hunger and fear. It has no moral. The scribe is a coward. The soldier is a fraud. The captain is a fool. They are chased by a thing they don’t understand to a place that shouldn’t exist.”

The Chronicler paused, his quill hovering. “This is irrelevant. Your personal narrative is being subsumed.”

“But here’s the thing,” Ulrich continued, as if he hadn’t heard. He walked forward, not with threat, but with the casual pace of a man settling by a campfire. “In this bad, messy story, they saw a walrus on a ship’s prow. And one of them, the coward, thought it looked lonely. So he gave it a friend. He carved it from a bit of broken wood. Not to worship. Not to sell. Just to say, ‘You are not alone.’ It was a useless thing. A waste of time and a knife’s edge.”

He was beside me now. He put a heavy, calloused hand on my shoulder. The touch was real. It anchored me. The Chronicler’s unwriting seemed to slough off it.

“That useless thing,” Ulrich said, his milky eye fixed on the Chronicler’s perfect face, “saved twenty-three men. It fought your ‘correction’ with a story of its own. A smaller story. A sillier one. But it held.”

The Chronicler’s expression tightened. “Sentimental contingency. A glitch. It will be smoothed.”

“Will it?” Ulrich asked. “You deal in the grand narrative. The Fall of Cities. The Patterns of Birds. The arcs of empires. What do you know of the weight of a dry cloak? The taste of shared cheese? The sound of a friend’s snore in the dark? You file our lives in your cubes. But you don’t read them.”

He was doing it. He was telling a story. Not to convince, not to fight. He was insisting. He was making our small, ragged truth present in this place of absolute abstraction. And the Archive, this perfect engine of order, had no protocol for it. It could delete, correct, file. It could not listen.

The Chronicler’s quill dipped again. He would write Ulrich out first. The instability was speaking.

I looked up. I saw the vast, silent machinery of the Archive. I saw the paused Captain, a statue of fury. I saw my friend, the old fraud, speaking his truth to the void. And I saw the desk. The one human thing.

I did not stand. I crawled. The distance to the desk was infinite, but the hook in my soul was taut, and it was not pulling me forward now. It was pulling the world toward me. I reached the leg of the desk. The dark wood. I put my hand on it. It felt like the rail of the Walrus. It felt like the table in the Wittenberg scriptorium.

The Chronicler saw me. He made a slight, dismissive gesture with his free hand.

A force like a silent wind tried to blow me back. It was not pain. It was the sensation of being erased from the current paragraph.

The wooden walrus in my tunic burned. It was not just singing now; it was shouting. A shout of absurd, stubborn existence.

I pulled the knife from my belt. Not the Chronicler’s quill of light. My knife. The one I’d used to carve. The edge was dull from cutting rope and bread. It was stained.

I did not look at the Chronicler. I looked at the desk. At its smooth, dark surface.

And I began to carve.

Not a word. Not a rune. A line. A deep, gouging, ugly scar in the perfect wood. The sound was a scream in the silence. It was the sound of the real, the imperfect, the done, violating the plane of the theoretical.

The Chronicler flinched. An actual, physical recoil. “Stop,” he said, his voice losing its flatness, gaining a sharp edge of something like horror. “You cannot… that is not a substrate for narrative! It is a tool!”

I carved another line, crossing the first. An ‘X’. A mark. My mark. Thomas of Wittenberg was here.

The Archive around us shuddered. The perfect light wavered. The cubes on their shelves flickered. My crude cut was a virus in the system. It was an event that was not an event. It was graffiti on the margin of the master copy.

“You are introducing chaos!” the Chronicler cried, standing now. His quill of light blazed, but it was useless. He could write me out of the story, but he could not unwrite the cut in the desk. It existed. It was a fact. A bad, messy, human fact.

Ulrich saw it. He laughed, a great, booming roar that shattered the last of the chamber’s holy quiet. “He’s not writing a story, you fool! He’s making a mark!”

The Chronicler stared at the defaced desk, at the raw, splintered wood. His bland face was a mask of confusion, then of dawning, terrible understanding. We were not characters to be corrected. We were vandals. And you cannot correct a vandal. You can only clean up the mess they leave behind.

He looked from the desk, to me, to Ulrich, to the paused Rensfeld. The entire, vast, silent weight of the Archive seemed to hold its breath, waiting for his command.

He did not give it.

He slowly, carefully, set down his quill of light. He closed the folio before him. He straightened his robe of un-inked vellum.

“You have contaminated the rectification chamber,” he said, his voice restored to its dry, precise tone, but now it was laced with a final, icy verdict. “You have introduced an uncategorisable variable. A physical alteration to a non-physical interface. The protocols… there are no protocols for this.”

He looked at me, and for a second, I saw not a god, not a functionary, but a librarian faced with a book that has spontaneously burst into flames on the shelf. “You cannot be integrated. The anomaly is now endemic. To pursue further correction would risk propagating the instability into adjacent narrative strata.”

He waved a hand. Rensfeld gasped, stumbling forward as his pause was released. He drew his cutlass, his eyes wild.

“Put that away, Captain,” the Chronicler said, weary now. “Your storm remains written. Your pain is still a line in the chronicle. I am not undoing it. I am… ceasing engagement. You are quarantined.”

He turned his back on us. He looked at the scarred desk, his shoulders slumping slightly. “You have your cessation, scribe. Not the one I intended. You will not become nothing. You will become… irrelevant. To us. You will return to your world. You will live out your noisy, pointless, messy little stories. You will die, unremarked and unfiled. You will be a story that is not told. The greatest silence we can afford you.”

He gestured, and the chamber, the Archive, the plain—they all began to fade, not into darkness, but into a kind of attentive indifference.

“Get out,” the Chronicler said, his voice already sounding distant. “And take your damn mark with you.”

The world inverted.

Not with a bang, but with the feeling of being dismissed.

We were back on the deck of the Walrus. The grey-white plain was gone. The Archive was gone. We were afloat on a calm, ordinary sea, under a high, pale sun. The hum was gone. The hook in my soul was… quiet. Not gone. Asleep.

The crew rushed around us, babbling questions we could not answer. Rensfeld leaned on the rail, breathing hard, staring at his hands as if seeing them for the first time.

Ulrich put his arm around my shoulders. “Well,” he said. “We’re not corrected.”

“We’re not anything,” I whispered, the weight of the dismissal crushing me. “We’re irrelevant. A story no one will ever read.”

He squeezed me. “Then we’re free, brother. The most free men in all creation. The authors of the only story that matters: our own.”

I reached into my tunic. The wooden walrus was there. It was cool now. Just wood. The glow was gone. Its story had been told. It had done its job.

I looked at the horizon. There was no pull. No hum. No destination.

Rensfeld turned to us, his face a map of exhausted wonder. “Where to now, scribe?”

I opened my mouth. I had no answer. West was a direction, not a purpose. The margin of the world was behind us. We had been written into the white space beyond the text.

Then I felt it. Not a hook. A whisper. A faint, familiar scent on the salt air. Not woodsmoke and dung. Something sharper. Ink. And damp stone. And the iron tang of a river.

I looked at Ulrich. He smelled it too. His good eye widened.

It was the smell of home. Not as a place to return to, but as a place to begin again. We had sailed to the end of the chronicle and been turned away. We had become a footnote that refused to be placed. We were an error that had learned to read, to write, and finally, to erase itself from the ledger.

But an error still exists. It still takes up space. It still breathes.

“East,” I said, my voice rough but sure. “Sail east, Captain.”

Rensfeld nodded, a slow, deep acknowledgement. He did not ask why. The question had died in the Archive. He turned to the helm. “East it is.”

The Walrus came about, her sails luffing then filling with a wind that was just wind, in a world that was, for us, just a world. We were irrelevant. We were free.

Ulrich and I stood together at the stern, watching the wake of our impossible journey fade into the blue.

“A better story?” he asked, echoing his question from a lifetime ago.

I thought of the scar on the dark wood desk in the sky. A mark that meant nothing to anyone but us. A signature in the void.

“Our story,” I said.

And as the ship settled on her new, old course, the rain began to fall. It was not God’s own punctuation. It was just rain. Cold, wet, and real. It found the gap between my hood and my neck with a pilgrim’s own persistence.

Ahead, somewhere beyond the curve of the sea, the city walls would be waiting. Not broken teeth, but just stones. The story was over. The story was just beginning.

We were sailing home. To write it.
